Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Stellar oscillations can probe the interior structure of stars, thus providing an understand-
ing of these which is impossible to achieve otherwise. Am and Fm stars are chemically
peculiar stars that experience diffusion due to slow rotation. Such stars were not expected
to be able to pulsate, but observations have proven that this is the case. Turbulent pres-
sure in the HeI/HI ionisation layer is thought to be the cause of this. In this project, 1287
Am and Fm stars are studied using TESS lightcurves and Gaia astrometry to charac-
terise stellar oscillations in these unexpected pulsators. It is found that 20% of Am stars
pulsate. However, theoretical predictions of the number of excited radial orders do not
coincide with the observations, suggesting that the models based on turbulent pressure
fail to describe the pulsations in Am and Fm stars completely.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars

==PAGE:9==
1
Introduction
The study of stellar oscillations enables the characterisation of the interior of stars, pro-
viding valuable information that otherwise cannot be retrieved using other observation
methods. Since the advent of helioseismology, successfully constraining the interior of
the Sun, the study has been extended to other types of stars, from main sequence O,B,A,F
stars to white dwarfs [1]. While there are still many unknowns regarding these, astero-
seismology has proved valuable in understanding stellar structure and evolution. In recent
years, space-borne photometry from Kepler and TESS missions have provided long, high
precision time series and allowed for the discovery of pulsation in many stars and rev-
olutionised asteroseismic modelling of these. This has enabled characterisation of the
interior for stars other than the Sun. In particular, TESS [2] provides an all-sky survey of
the brightest stars with short, 2 minute cadence. The wealth of data is valuable to describe
the different populations of stars and further constrain the physical processes within.
Last year, a new Gaia data release was made available, providing unprecedented high-
quality astrometry for more than a billion stars and estimates for many stellar parameters
[3]. This large amount of data is valuable for target selection and combined analysis with
other data sources, and may be particularly powerful to characterise large samples.
Am and Fm stars are chemically peculiar stars characterised by an under-abundance
of Calcium primarily, and over-abundances of metallic elements [4]. They owe these pe-
culiarities to atomic diffusion, a process involving gravitational settling of certain elements
and radiative levitation of metals with rich absorption spectra. This is expected to hinder
stellar oscillations as it leads to He depletion in the HeII ionisation layer, which drives p-
mode pulsations through the κ-mechanism. Yet, a significant fraction of Am stars have
been observed to pulsate, thus challenging current knowledge of excitation mechanism.
Turbulent pressure in the H/HeI ionisation layer is proposed to be responsible for pulsa-
tions in Am stars and simulations of the number of excited radial orders of p-modes have
been computed [5]. No observational data has been collected and analysed in order to
compare with these models, and very little studies of Am stars have been carried out to
date using TESS satellite data. In this project, it is proposed to study the pulsational be-
haviour of a large sample of Am stars from the Renson catalog [6] using TESS and Gaia
data, in order to characterise this population of stars. It will provide the community with
key statistics on different aspects, such as pulsation incidence in Am stars, as well as
help testing whether turbulent pressure can explain the observed pulsations.
An overview of current theory regarding the chemically peculiar Am stars as well as rele-
vant information regarding stellar evolution and oscillations and the fundamental principles
for frequency analysis are provided in chapter 2. In chapter 3, the required data for this
study is presented, followed by an explanation of the methods and assumptions used in
chapter 4. Results are presented in chapter 5. A discussion of certain limitations and
potential future work and conclusions based on the outcome of the analysis are provided
in chapters 6 and 7, respectively.

2
Theory
This chapter covers the fundamental theoretical concepts for understanding the contents
of the report. The first sections are dedicated to the general description of stars, their
fundamental properties and evolution, as well as the peculiarities of the target stars in this
project, the Am stars. The rest of the chapter presents the essentials of asteroseismology,
and the mathematical tools that enable the analysis of stellar oscillations.
2.1
Overview of Stars
Stars are astronomical objects consisting of gases, for the most part ionised, and emit
electromagnetic radiation through fusion of chemical elements in their core. They consist
predominantly of hydrogen and helium, the two most abundant elements in the universe,
although other heavier elements can be detected.
Stars are in hydrostatic equilibrium, and as such the temperature and density of the gases
composing the stellar structure increase with depth from the surface. Near the centre, re-
ferred to as the core of the star, the star is hot enough for nuclear fusion to occur [7]. The
gravitational pressure exerted by the gas is countered by the outward pressure originating
from the energy released in its core. The energy is carried from the core to the surface
of the star through different modes of transport. The primary energy transport is photon
radiation, where photons are emitted and migrate towards the surface through absorp-
tion and reemission [7]. Radiation escapes the star through emission into space from a
layer of relatively low density called the photosphere, which thus is defined as the outer
surface of the star. Convection of gases also occurs in regions of high opacity, meaning
that photons cannot travel effectively though the medium, or when the amount of energy
to be transported is very large [8]. Similarly to any fluid, hot gas expands and has lower
density, thus rising through parts of the star, while colder gas sinks. Energy is therefore
transported outwards through motion of the matter in the star. These convective layers
only extend through a certain region of the star and contribute to mixing the elements
within [4]. Simplistically, high-mass stars have convective cores while low-mass stars
have convective envelopes, a term used to describe the region outside of the core.
Because of the physical properties of stars, and the distances at which they are from
our Solar System, we are constrained to only study the radiation they emit in order to
learn more about these. Observing a star through photometry or spectroscopy thus cor-
responds to studying the light originating from its surface, and its interactions with the other
components of the stellar atmosphere - gases in outer layers from the photosphere. The
radiation emitted into space has undergone various transformation during the travel from
the stellar core and its surface and does not carry much information about the conditions
in the star’s interior [7]. This therefore limits the understanding of the inner regions of stars.
From observations, a handful of stellar parameters are typically derived to characterise
the star. Some of these are described below.
2.1.1
Effective Temperature
In order to model stars and their emission, some simplifications are commonly made.
The first is to assume that they are blackbodies, which absorb electromagnetic radiation
in an idealised, perfect, manner at all wavelengths [9]. They emit radiation according to
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
3
==PAGE:12==
Planck’s law, which depends on the temperature T.
Bλ(λ) = 2hc2
λ5
1
e
hc
λkBT −1
(2.1)
Equation 2.1 describes the spectral radiance, the power emitted by a blackbody as a func-
tion of wavelength. From this a star can be characterised by a surface effective tempera-
ture, Teff, that would produce a blackbody with the same amount of radiation. Depending
on the effective temperature, the radiation at a given wavelength will vary. Figure 2.1 illus-
trates the difference in the Planck curves for stars with different effective temperatures.
The effective temperatures are 18000 K, 6000 K and 4000 K respectively for the blue,
yellow and red curves. Stars radiate strongly in the visible range of the electromagnetic
spectrum in all cases, but large amounts of infrared radiation can be detected as well. For
the hotter stars, ultraviolet light also is significant. From hereon, focus will remain on the
visible range of the electromagnetic spectrum.
Figure 2.1: Example blackbody Planck curves for stars with different effective tempera-
tures. Credit: Swinburne University.
2.1.2
Apparent Magnitude & Luminosity
Stars are to first order considered spherical, implying that radiation is emitted in all direc-
tions. As it travels through space, the light passes through an increasingly large surface
area and the energy for a given area decreases with distance. This is described by the
inverse-square law in Equation 2.2, which relates how the total luminosity L of a star
decreases with distance d [7].
b =
L
4πd2
(2.2)
When observing a star from Earth, one therefore measures its apparent brightness, b,
which represents the amount of energy passing through a surface area at a given distance.
Originally, stars were classified according to how bright they appeared in the night sky.
The apparent magnitude is a unit-less scale describing how bright a star appears to be as
seen from Earth, and in its modern form can be computed from the apparent brightness
of a star as seen in Equation 2.3.
m = −2.5 log10(b) + c
(2.3)
4
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:13==
However, the apparent magnitude does not tell anything about the true nature of the star,
as an intrinsically more luminous star located further away from Earth may appear fainter
than a star close by. It is therefore useful to relate the brightness to the luminosity, which
requires knowledge of the distance to the star, as can be seen in Equation 2.2. For sim-
plicity and comparison capabilities, this quantity is often expressed in terms of the solar
luminosity, where stars are assigned a value L⊙based on the fraction between their own
luminosity and that of the Sun. Similarly to the apparent magnitude scale, the absolute
magnitude scale is computed from the luminosity of a star and enables comparison of the
magnitude of different stars. It essentially corresponds to the apparent magnitude of the
given star at a distance of 10 pc [8].
Distances
Distances can be estimated in the simplest way by measuring the parallax of a star [7].
As Earth completes its orbit, the motion of the planet means that the line of sight from an
observer to a star shifts. Consequently, the star appears to be moving in the night sky.
This motion can be described by an angle, called the stellar parallax p, and can be used
to infer the distance of a star by using Equation 2.4.
d = 1
p
(2.4)
Here the parallax is given in arcseconds and the distance in parsecs. An obvious limitation
appears from this relation, as it requires that the parallax be determined with extremely
high precision for this method to yield a reliable distance value. As stars are further away
from Earth, the stellar parallax decreases significantly and distances to such stars cannot
be determined reliably [7].
2.1.3
Spectral Classification
The MK Spectral Classification System is based on classifying stars by looking at their
spectra, which represent the light intensity as a function of wavelength, in the blue-violet
region of the visible spectrum [4]. Absorption lines (also simply referred to as lines) can be
found in the spectra. Visually, they appear as dips in intensity and represent interactions
of the light emitted from the photosphere and the gases in the star’s atmosphere. The MK
system uses these to identify patterns and group stars with the same properties, without
making any prior assumptions regarding theory of the nature of the star, thus serving as
a reliable way of grouping stars in classes according to similar characteristics [4]. The
terminology ”metal lines” is used to qualify any line originating from elements other than
hydrogen and helium. Classification is performed by comparing a spectrum with spectra
of standard stars each representing a different class to identify the common lines, and
then assigning the label corresponding to the best match. Stars are typically assigned
labels O, B, A, F, G, K or M depending on their spectra and serves as a temperature
scale, with the hottest stars being assigned class O and the coolest stars being from
class M. The effective temperature has the most impact on the spectrum of a star, as all
have roughly the same chemical composition dominated by hydrogen and helium, with
only 1% of heavier elements [7], [8]. Because of the near-identical compositions, higher
temperature stars also are the ones with larger masses. A summary of the properties of
these spectral classes is provided in Table 2.1.

Table 2.1: Stellar spectral classes with corresponding temperature [8] and mass ranges
[10].
The classes are subdivided by a number ranging from 0 to 9 based on the strength of the
absorption lines, with hotter stars having a smaller number, for more detailed classifica-
tion. One spectral class therefore corresponds to a letter and number association. Early
and late-type stars are used to qualify stars with spectral classes corresponding to hotter
and cooler stars, respectively.
Surface Gravity
Additionally, stars are assigned a luminosity class I-V depending on the width of their
absorption lines, and thus helps distinguish stars further [8]. The surface gravity refers
to the acceleration of a particle at the surface of a given star. It is closely related to the
radius of the star, as a large radius leads to a low surface gravity. Because more radiation
is emitted from a sphere with larger surface area, a larger radius is also correlated to a
higher luminosity. The luminosity classes are therefore ordered as follows:
I. Supergiant
II. Bright giants
III. Giants
IV. Subgiants
V. Main sequence star
These classes represent a sequence of increasing surface gravity and decreasing lumi-
nosity. Main sequence stars, which will be described in the following section and are of
most interest in this project, have the luminosity class V. They are also referred to as
dwarfs. As an example, the Sun is a main sequence star and has the spectral classifica-
tion G2V. This study focuses on A and F type stars. For this reason, a short description
of some key characteristics for these spectral classes is provided below.
A-type Stars
A-type stars have mostly radiative atmospheres and shallow convection zones in the en-
velope. Temperature estimates can most effectively be made by looking at the strength of
Balmer lines, a specific set of hydrogen energy transitions, and the Ca II K-line [4], where
the roman numeral refers to the ionisation state of the element. The mentioned lines are
strongest in A-type stars compared to other classes and serve as ideal tools to identify
the spectral class. The Ca II K-line is particularly useful as it gets stronger for later A-type
stars and can be used to classify the entire range from A0 to A9. Metal lines, for example
those related to Iron (Fe) and Titanium (Ti), grow stronger for later spectral types as well.
F-type Stars
F-type stars show a transition in the stellar atmospheres from A-type stars. The convec-
tion regions become deeper, eventually being the major energy transport mechanism by
6
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:15==
late F-type stars and early G-type stars. Both hydrogen and metal lines can be used for
temperature estimates, although the Ca II K-line seems insensitive to the different sub-
classes in F-type stars [4].
2.1.4
Magnetic Fields & Spots
Magnetic fields are a widespread phenomenon, from small-scale fields powered by ob-
jects on Earth to large magnetic fields around planets and stars and with extremely vari-
able strengths. Late spectral type stars such as our Sun have deep convective regions
that power dynamo magnetic fields [11]. In O, B and A stars, roughly 10% are thought to
be magnetic [12]. These are of fossil origin, i.e. remnant magnetic fields that originated
during stellar formation. Observations of A-type stars has also enabled detection of ultra-
weak magnetic fields, although they are extremely difficult to measure [13]. F-type stars
are known to host the transition from shallow convective outer layers in higher mass stars
to deep convective regions seen in low mass, late type stars. Within this class magnetic
fields have also been detected, with no clear correlation to the effective temperature [11].
Magnetic fields are expected to prohibit mixing within the star, or to be able to affect
the chemical composition of the stellar atmospheres [12]. If they are strong enough, it is
possible for them to induce diffusion along the field lines, resulting in enhancements of
certain elements. Additionally, magnetic fields are known to provoke the appearance of
stellar spots, the best studied example being those on our Sun. They occur in a region
where the magnetic field is strong enough to guide the ionised gases of the star outward.
This region is located further away and is therefore cooler, hence less bright [7]. Such
spots can cause periodic variations in the stellar brightness and can thus be identified
from lightcurves.
2.2
Stellar Evolution
Unless stated specifically, the contents of this section are based on [8]. Stars are evolv-
ing celestial objects. They form in gas clouds as these begin to contract, allowing for
the matter to gather and heat up. When the temperature is high enough at the center
of this protostar, nuclear fusion of hydrogen into helium can occur. This process liber-
ates energy, which in turn is radiated into space. Contraction of the star is halted, as the
star now finds itself in hydrostatic equilibrium. The star spends most of its lifetime in the
hydrogen fusion phase, referred to as the main sequence. The length of this phase is
highly dependent on mass, with larger stars fusing hydrogen into helium at higher rates
and thus having shorter lifetimes. The onset of hydrogen burning defines the zero-age
main sequence (ZAMS). Terminal-age main sequence (TAMS) corresponds to the end of
hydrogen fusion in the core.
Eventually, all hydrogen is consumed in the core. The star begins to contract, enabling
hydrogen fusion in a shell around the core. This happens in a gradually increasing region
outside of the core and causes the outer layers to expand. The star’s luminosity increases
but the outer layers cool and the star enters the red giant phase [7].
Ultimately, the temperature in the core reaches the threshold allowing for helium fusion,
producing carbon and oxygen. As a consequence, the core expands and the outer layers
contract, leading to an increase in temperature and slight decrease in luminosity [7]. The
evolution after the main sequence is beyond the scope of this project and will therefore
not be covered in detail. It is worth noting that depending on the mass of the star, fusion of
successively larger elements, such as carbon, neon, oxygen and silicon can occur in the
core while shell fusion of the previous elements remains present. Star sustaining such
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
7
==PAGE:16==
processes enter the supergiant phase.
It follows from the above explanations that the properties of the star vary as they evolve.
The observable surface properties can be used to study the stars and learn about their
current stage in evolution. The Hertzsprung-Russel (HR) diagrams are graphs in which
stars are represented by their luminosity and effective temperature. HR diagrams are
valuable tools to study stars in terms of their intrinsic properties, as they are not randomly
distributed and the location on the HR diagram provides information on their evolutionary
state [7], as is illustrated in Figure 2.2. The departure from main sequence and transition
into the red giant phase results in the motion towards the upper right in the diagram.
Figure 2.2: Example HR diagram, also showing the stellar radius for different portions of
the graph. The main sequence is shown as a red line. Taken from [7].
Binary Systems
Binary systems are star systems involving two stars that are gravitationally bound. Unre-
solved binary systems pose a major challenge to the study of stars. They appear to be
a single star but the measured light is in fact the combination of both sources. As a con-
sequence, they will appear higher in the HR diagram [8]. The colour information is also
an average of that from the two stars and therefore binarity can affect the temperature
estimates of photometric methods involving colour filters. This is relevant when analysing
stars in the HR diagram.
Binary systems can be detected in different ways. Eclipsing binaries are systems ob-
served from a specific angle, where the transit from one star in front of the other results
in a decrease in brightness in photometric time series. Spectroscopic binaries are found
due to Doppler shifts in absorption lines. Finally, astrometric binaries are systems in which
only one star is visible but the motion is perturbed, which follows from gravitational inter-
actions with a companion star.
8
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:17==
In close binary systems, mass transfer can occur as one star goes through its evolu-
tion and the radius expands [14]. The matter from the star may overflow the Roche lobe,
the region in space where it is gravitationally bound to the star, and be capture by the
companion. The chemical composition of stars can be affected by this.
2.3
Chemically Peculiar Stars
Although a reliable stellar classification method based on stellar spectra has been de-
veloped, many stars display unusual characteristics and their spectra deviate from those
of the standard stars. Some stars show unexpected weaker or stronger absorption lines
when compared to stars of a similar spectral class, resulting in either smaller or higher
abundance estimates of certain elements in their photosphere. Consequently, these are
referred to chemically peculiar stars, because their chemical abundances are different
from those of the class they are associated to. In the case of A-type stars, 30% show
such signs and a wide variety of peculiarities have been identified, making it an important
phenomenon to understand and characterise. Unless stated otherwise, the contents of
this section are based on [4].
2.3.1
Am & Fm Stars
Some A-type stars show over-abundances of metallic elements in their atmosphere. Am
stars, also referred to as metallic-lined A stars, are defined as stars having under-abundances
of calcium (Ca) and scandium (Sc), as well as over-abundances of zinc (Zn), strontium
(Sr), yttrium (Y), zirconium (Zr) and barium (Ba) [15]. With the Ca II K-line appearing weak
in these stars, suggesting an early spectral subclass, but other metal lines being strong,
the spectral types derived using these sets of lines differ. For the star to be confirmed as
an Am star, it would need the spectral subclasses to differ by at least 5. As an example,
an Am star could have a Ca-lines corresponding to a spectral class A2 and metal-lines
associated with an A7 star. Should the difference be less than 5 subclasses, the stars are
called marginal Am stars [16]. This phenomenon has also been observed in F-type stars,
where the same principles apply. Because the spectral class differs depending on which
elements are studied, a new notation was developed specifically for Am stars. As can
be seen in the example below [16], an Am star is typical given multiple spectral classes,
based on the K-line spectral type and the metallic line type (m).
A3/5mF0−F5
The peculiarities found in the atmospheres of Am & Fm stars are believed to originate
from a chemical separation in the outer layers of the stars that does not normally take
place for A and F type stars. This process is referred to as atomic diffusion [16]. It con-
sists of two effects, gravitational settling of elements and radiative levitating. In principle,
heavier elements have a tendency to sink into the star as a consequence of the gravita-
tional force, however radiation pressure from the inner part of the star will be larger for an
ionised element with a rich spectrum [17]. By rich spectrum it is implied that the element
has many absorption lines, so that interactions with photons are numerous. The momen-
tum from the photon is transferred to the element upon absorption and as a consequence,
these elements will rise in the atmosphere of the star and chemical peculiarities can arise.
Metals have rich spectra in the ultraviolet region of light, whilst lighter elements such as
helium and calcium do not. The Fm peculiarity can only occur for early F-type stars (down
to F5), as convection becomes more prominent in later spectral classes and helps mix the
elements, hence impeding the Fm phenomenon.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
9
==PAGE:18==
Stars are required to be slowly rotating in order to experience atomic diffusion, with ro-
tational velocities being less than 120 km/s [18]. Rotating stars experience large-scale
circulation in the envelope and this mixing prevents the formation of peculiarities. It was
found that the vast majority of Am stars are slow rotators [17]. Several reasons could
explain how this can occur for A and F type stars. Tidal interactions with another star in
a binary system can cause a rotational slowdown and the development of chemical pe-
culiarities. Statistically, roughly 60-70% of Am stars are estimated to be in binaries [19].
This does leave a significant amount of Am stars to be slowly rotating outside of binary
systems. Another reason could be of magnetic origin, where a strong magnetic field is
thought to break the rotation. However, Am stars have not been found to have magnetic
fields capable of such a phenomenon. However, stellar spots have been observed on Am
stars [15], which are believed to be formed in the presence of magnetic fields or an un-
known mechanism. Ultra-weak magnetic fields have been confirmed in several Am stars
with effective temperatures 9200-9500 K, although it remains unconfirmed whether this
is a feature common to the entire class [20, 21]. Finally, it seems there is evidence for
some Am stars being born with slow rotation [19].
ρ Puppis stars
ρ-Puppis stars, named after the prototype star of this class, are mid F-type stars that show
similar chemical peculiarities to those found in Am & Fm stars [22]. They appear to have
luminosity types II-III, and are believed to be evolved Am stars in the transition phase
where the convective layers deepen, ultimately removing the chemical peculiarities found
in the outer layers. One major limitation to this is that atomic diffusion is not expected to
be able to occur in mid to late type F stars. A proposed alternative is that the observed
abundances are developed through accretion from a companion star, similarly to Barium
stars [22].
2.3.2
Ap Stars
Ap stars, or peculiar A stars, represent another category of chemically peculiar stars. They
have strong magnetic fields, which is moreover expected to explain why they also are are
slow rotators [23]. Diffusion is therefore expected to be the cause of the peculiarities in
these stars as well. They consist of over-abundances of chromium (Cr) and strontium
(Sr), as well as rare-earth elements [23, 24], which differ from metal elements by their
high atomic numbers. Examples are neodymium (Nd) and europium (Eu). Ap stars have
effective temperatures belonging to B-type stars, but can be as cool as F-type stars.
The effect of magnetic fields can be seen as absorption lines belonging to specific el-
ements in Ap stellar spectra, have been found to vary periodically in strength. The varia-
tions can be different between chemical elements, implying that they are concentrated in
specific regions, or spots, on the stellar surface. Ions are sensible to the magnetic field
lines and therefore gather along these, resulting in the observed inhomogeneities.
2.3.3
λ Bootis Stars
λ Bootis stars are a class of generally metal-weak stars, with a consistently weak magne-
sium (Mg) absorption line. They are mostly of spectral classes corresponding to A-type
stars. They are expected to have normal rotational velocities as compared to normal A
stars and should be on or near the main sequence due to their broad hydrogen lines.
Under-abundances of metals in these stars is thought to be a consequence of accretion
of gases, which consist of the most common light elements and are metal-poor, as cir-
cumstellar disks have been observed in several cases.
10
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:19==
2.3.4
HgMn Stars
Mercury-manganese stars, or HgMn stars, are late B-type stars (spectral types B7 to B9)
with exceptionally strong absorption lines related to Hg II and Mn II. These are found in
the blue-violet region of the visible spectrum. Studies of their peculiarities have shown
similarities with those of Am stars, especially hot ones with early A-type hydrogen lines.
HgMn stars are also found in binary systems, with an estimated binary incidence of up to
67%.
2.4
Stellar Oscillations
As has been mentioned before, the observable light from stars is emitted at their surface
and carries little information about the interior conditions. So far, stars have been de-
scribed as generally invariant, except for the different evolutionary stages they go through,
over long time-scales (millions to billions of years). However, the light from stars has been
observed to vary in intensity over time. Time series showing the measured flux from the
star as a function of time, or simply lightcurves, have enabled discoveries of different types
of variability. Extrinsic variability is photometric variability unrelated to the star’s own prop-
erties. An example of this are periodic variations in light intensity induced by eclipses in
some binary systems. Intrinsic variability originates from the star itself. Variations in size
cause brightness changes, also referred to as pulsations or oscillations [25]. In relation
to stellar oscillations, the discipline of asteroseismology has emerged. It deals with the
determination of stellar interior structures from their oscillations, that are used as seismic
waves [25].
2.4.1
Theoretical Overview
Stellar oscillations perturb the physical state of the stars. Under the assumption that
stars are spherical in shape and neglecting rotation and magnetic fields, they can be
described with spherical harmonics [26]. The oscillations can be described through three
wavenumbers:
• l : degree
• m : azimuthal order
• n : radial orders, or overtone
Each unique combination of values for each of these corresponds to a specific oscillation
mode. An example of some modes can be found in Figure 2.3. In stars these are three-
dimensional, although the one-dimensional analogy of a string is helpful in understanding
the terminology. The radial order corresponds to the number of nodes, i.e. points where
the string is not oscillating, on a vibrating string. In the 3D case, these are no longer
points but shells within the star [26]. The degree describes the number of nodal lines
on the surface and the azimuthal order counts the number of these lines that cross the
star’s symmetry axis. Oscillations of degree l = 0 correspond to purely radial modes, i.e.
outward and inward motion of the stellar envelope. The modes shown in Figure 2.3 result
in regions of the star that move inwards (red) and other ones that are displaced outwards
(blue). Because of this, higher degree modes result in more complex, small scale varia-
tions. When observing at large distances as is the case from the Earth, such modes can
be difficult to detect because the flux measured from the point-like star corresponds to the
motion of the entire surface [26].
Oscillation modes are distinguished according to which force restores the star’s equilib-
rium state from these perturbations [26]. Two main forces are considered; pressure and
buoyancy. Pressure modes, or p-modes, are mainly restored from pressure and can be
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
11
==PAGE:20==
found in the stellar envelopes. Gravity modes, also called g-modes, are restored from the
buoyancy force, and occur in the deep interior of stars. The p-modes are found to have
strong radial motions whilst g-modes are horisontal modes. Moreover, modes of different
orders are able to penetrate at different depths in the star, with higher order modes having
only shallow penetration [27]. Identification of different pulsation modes therefore makes
it possible to infer properties of different regions of the stellar interior. Stars that evolve
can host mixed modes, which have p-mode characteristics in the envelope of the star but
behave as g-modes deep inside the star [26].
Figure 2.3:
Example radial components of non-radial oscillation modes for different
wavenumbers. Adapted from [26]. The degree and azimuthal modes (l, m) are given
row by row. Upper row: (1,0), (2,0), (3,0). Bottom row: (1,1), (2,2), (3,3). Blue (red)
regions are moving outwards (inwards).
2.4.2
Pulsation HR Diagram
In this section different classes of pulsating stars are presented. Figure 2.4 is a summary
of all types of pulsators. The red dotted lines represent stellar evolutionary tracks in order
to guide the eye, with the main sequence being shown as a solid red line. It is clear that
stellar oscillations can be found in stars in many stages of their lives, across the entire
HR diagram, although only a subset of these will be considered in this study. A review of
most pulsators can be found in [1].
A and F stars typically have masses in the range 1.2M⊙to 2.5M⊙. Pulsation of such
stars near the main sequence are found in the lower part of the instability strip, which
is shown as black dashed lines. It is a region where numerous types of oscillating stars
are found. The pulsating classes relevant for the study of Am stars are presented in the
following sections, with a quick overview of some key characteristics.
δ-Scuti
stars pulsate in p-modes, with frequencies typically above 4 cycles per day (c/d)
and reaching up to 80 c/d [1]. The oscillations are driven by the κ-mechanism, which oc-
curs in regions of a star where the opacity κ is high. This usually happens when there is at
least partial ionisation of a chemical element, which impedes the transport of energy from
the interior to the surface, ultimately forcing the outer layers to expand in order for the
accumulated energy to be released [25]. This cycle repeats in a valve-like mechanism,
resulting in the observed pulsations. The κ-mechanism is thought to operate in the He II
ionisation layer for δ-Scuti stars.
12
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:21==
Figure 2.4: HR diagram showing all classes of pulsating stars. Taken from [1].
δ-Scuti stars are the most common main sequence pulsating class and have diverse
pulsational behaviours [1]. Stars with only a handful of oscillation modes can be found
whereas others pulsate in complex manners with many modes spread over a large range
of observable frequencies. In some cases, stars with very different behaviours have simi-
lar stellar parameters, raising the question of whether an unknown mode selection mech-
anism is at work [1].
A sub-class of δ-Scuti stars are the high amplitude δ-Scuti (HADS) stars, which pulsate
in the radial fundamental (l=0) and first overtone modes [5, 28]. They appear to have
asymmetric lightcurves with high amplitude variations.
γ-Doradus
stars pulsate exclusively in g-modes, which mostly have low frequencies.
Convective flux modulation occurs deep inside the star and drives the g-mode oscillations.
At the base of a convection zone, the flux coming from the core is modulated by the
convective motion, which is thought to generate pulsations in the star [25, 29]. It is similar
to the κ-mechanism in the sense that the opacity at the boundary between the radiative
interior and the base of the convection zone is high. Observations of γ-Dor stars has
enable the study of multiple properties of stellar cores, such as internal rotation, mass
and radius [1].
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
13
==PAGE:22==
roAp
stars are rapidly oscillating Ap stars, a sub-group of the chemically peculiar Ap
stars that pulsate in high radial orders [5]. They enable studies of magnetic fields and
their effect on pulsation [1].
Hybrids
stars pulsate both in p-modes and g-modes, thus having characteristics of both
δ-Scuti and γ-Dor stars [30].
A significant number of stars show this dual behaviour
caused by supposedly two different excitation mechanisms. These stars defied the knowl-
edge of excitation mechanisms and are therefore targets of high interest to study and
understand these pulsations. Theoretical and observational instability strips have been
computed to delimit the region in the HR diagram where δ-Scuti pulsations occur [31],
and likewise for γ-Dor stars [32]. There is a slight overlap between these instability strips,
within which most hybrid stars can be expected to be found.
Am stars
experience diffusion, leading to the sinking of helium and depletion of the
He II ionisation layer. For this reason, Am stars were not expected to pulsate as the κ-
mechanism cannot excite oscillations. However, observations of these have revealed that
stellar oscillations do occur in a significant fraction. Turbulent pressure from the combined
H I and He I ionization layer is believed to contribute to excitation of p-modes [33]. It is
likely present in all δ-Scuti stars, being most prominent for stars with Teff ≈7500K, but
has been proposed as the main mechanism behind Am & Fm pulsations [5].
2.4.3
Asteroseismology
Asteroseismology is the discipline related to modelling the interior of stars using their
identified oscillation modes, much like seismic waves on Earth have enabled a mapping
of its structure. Asteroseismology has enabled accurate calculations of stellar masses and
age [5]. It requires a precise identification of stellar oscillation modes. Detailed reviews of
the methods and applications within asteroseismology can be found [26, 27, 34], although
modelling of stars is beyond the scope of this project. Therefore, no further detail will be
provided on this topic.
2.5
Time Series Analysis
Stars can oscillate in multiple modes and all of these contribute to the global variation
in the stellar brightness over time. These are difficult to identify in the lightcurves, but
can effectively be separated in frequency, as each mode is associated with a specific
frequency and amplitude. The principles of time series analysis will be provided in the
following sections and are based on [34].
2.5.1
Fourier Transform
Periodic variations in a time series can be analysed with Fourier transforms. A periodic
signal is represented with the function shown in Equation 2.5.
v(t) = a0 cos(ω0t −δ0)
(2.5)
a0 is the amplitude of the signal, δ0 is the phase, and ω0 is the angular frequency, which
when divided by 2π yields the oscillation frequency ν0, expressed in cycles per day (c/d)
or micro-Hertz (µHz). The Fourier transform of such a function over an interval of time
[0, T] is shown in Equation 2.6.
V (ω) =
! T
0
v(t)eiωt
(2.6)

of the sinc function [34].
Figure 2.6: Example amplitude spectrum generated from sim-

0.1 c/d with amplitudes 2 and 3, respectively.
The product of this transform is a spectrum, which describes the frequency content of the
signal. In the considered case, it results in two signals, centered at ν0 and −ν0. Typi-
cally, only the part of the spectrum with positive frequencies is considered, as the two
components are well separated (Tν0 >> 1) and therefore the information is identical both
at positive and negative values. Because the time interval is finite, the terms resulting
from the Fourier transform contain sinc functions, which are defined in Equation 2.7. The
shape of a sinc function is plotted in the frequency domain in Figure 2.5. It consists of a
main peak centered at the frequency ν0 and multiple side lobes of decreasing amplitudes
on both sides.
sinc(x) = sin(x)
x
(2.7)
In the case of stellar oscillations, it is assumed that the time series consists of a linear
combination of periodic signals like the one in Equation 2.5. The Fourier transform of such
a time series would result in a spectrum containing multiple peaks at the frequencies of
oscillation. They can be expected to have similar shapes as shown in Figure 2.5, although
additional processes such as noise, variations in amplitude and aliasing, affect the peaks.
2.5.2
Amplitude Spectra
When analysing pulsation peaks, the amplitude spectrum |V (ω)| is most commonly con-
sidered. Figure 2.6 shows one such spectrum. It was computed from a synthetic lightcurve
consisting of two sine functions with two oscillation frequencies at 0.09 and 0.1 cycles per
day. White Gaussian noise was added to the signal to illustrate the expected effect in real
data. The simulated oscillations have amplitudes of 2 and 3 respectively for the lower
and higher frequency. The noise contributes to the spectrum with a random oscillation at
all frequencies, which can result in suppression or increase of the amplitude. As can be
seen in Figure 2.6, the amplitude of the two peaks does not correspond exactly to their
true values. Because noise is random, the effect is reduced compared to the contribution
from stellar oscillations, resulting in higher signal-to-noise ratios (S/N) for longer observa-
tions.
The shape of the spectrum depends strongly on the sampling frequency of the data.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
15
==PAGE:24==
Lightcurves consist of data points that result from the integration of the stellar flux over a
certain amount of time and are therefore not continuous functions. The oscillations from
the star are therefore sampled at a specific frequency fs. The Nyquist frequency is de-
fined as the highest frequency that can be resolved unambiguously, and can be found in
Equation 2.8.
Nf = fs
2
(2.8)
The frequency range of the amplitude spectrum is therefore limited up until the Nyquist fre-
quency. Higher frequencies present in a signal that is sampled at fs experience aliasing,
distorting the spectrum with artificial peaks, called aliases, spaced periodically at harmon-
ics of the original frequency and affecting the region below the Nyquist frequency [35]. In
order to study δ-Scuti pulsating stars, which most commonly pulsate up to 80 c/d, a high
Nf is therefore required.
 
When observing targets, whether it be from space or on Earth, data cannot be acquired
continuously due to orbits and target selection criteria in large surveys. This introduces
gaps in the time series, which cause aliasing in the amplitude spectrum. An example is
shown in Figure 2.7. The graph is centered around an oscillation frequency, which now
consists of multiple alias peaks of decreasing amplitudes. The effect is more prominent
for larger gaps, but is reduced when combining more data. It is seen from this figure that
aliasing can complicate the identification of the exact frequency of pulsation.
Figure 2.7: Example of effect of aliasing in amplitude spectrum as a consequence of large
data gaps. A single peak contains multiple alias peaks which complicates the identifica-
tion. The difference between two peaks indicates a data gap of roughly 1 year.
16
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:25==
Other important properties of the spectrum are the frequency resolution of the spectrum,
δν, and the smallest frequency difference between two resolvable peaks, which depend
on the length of the observation T, as seen in Equation 2.9.
δω ≈2π
T , δν ≈1
T
(2.9)
A theoretical minimum frequency separation can be derived based on the duration of
observation T, corresponding to the smallest frequency difference between two resolvable
peaks. It is provided in Equation 2.10 and essentially corresponds to twice the peak width
δω in Equation 2.9.
δν = 12
T
(2.10)
To illustrate these principles, artificial lightcurves of different lengths were generated. In
Figure 2.6, the time series was simulated for 10000 seconds. A shorter simulation of
1000 seconds was also carried out to illustrate the effect on the computed spectrum. The
figure can be found in section A.1. The consequence is exactly that reflected by the above
equations: the width of the peaks is increased and the frequency resolution is poorer for
shorter times T. Because certain peaks can be quite wide if the oscillations are recorded
for a relatively short period of time and the amplitudes between different modes may vary
significantly, resolving peaks may prove impossible. If a star contains two modes with
frequencies very close to each other, one mode may end up completely or partially hiding
the signal of the second mode in the spectrum if its amplitude is considerably larger.
This is due to the fact that side-lobes of the large-amplitude pulsation also scale with
the amplitude of the oscillation, and may therefore overshadow the second peak. It was
reproduced by simulating a signal with two oscillations with frequencies at 0.1 and 0.95
c/d and amplitudes of 5 and 1, respectively. The spectrum can be found in section A.1.
2.5.3
Mode Identification
In order to characterise stellar oscillations, it is important to identify the pulsation modes
present in the spectrum. This can prove challenging, as δ-Scuti stars have rich, highly
variable pulsation spectra from one star to another. These oscillation frequencies can be
of weak amplitude, making it difficult to systematically look for modes [36]. High order
g-modes often are very closely spaced in frequency and therefore need long time series
of at least 90 days to be resolved in γ-Dor [1]. Despite this, some important principles
enable the study of stellar oscillations in a first approach, and are presented below.
Asymptotic Relations
Some fundamental properties have been derived for p-modes and g-modes. Firstly, for
increasing orders n of a p-mode, the frequencies increase. On the contrary, the g-modes
decrease in frequency for increasing order [27]. In the asymptotic regime, i.e. for high
orders of a certain pulsation degree, pressure modes are spaced equally in their frequen-
cies. The distance between successive orders is called the large frequency separation. It
can be used to infer the mass of the star [27]. In the asymptotic regime of g-modes, these
are spaced equally according to the periods. It allows to study the region near the core,
if there is a long enough sequence of g-mode overtones [1].
Combination Frequencies and Harmonics
When identifying oscillation modes, the aim is to determine which peaks in a spectrum
are independent and hence truly associated with pulsation. Some peaks are combina-
tion frequencies, which can be written as a linear combination of observed independent
frequencies fi in Equation 2.11.
fc =
"
cifi
(2.11)
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
17
==PAGE:26==
ci can be a positive or negative integer. The combination order, O is given by the sum of
the values of each constant, as shown in Equation 2.12 [37].
Oj =
"
i̸=j
|ci|
(2.12)
Independent frequencies have order 0, indistinguishable frequencies have order 1 and
combination frequencies have order 2 or greater. Harmonics are a subset of the latter, as
they are located at multiples of a single frequency and thus only have one constant c > 0.
These originate from non-sinusoidal variations [37] and are common in eclipsing binary
systems, where the majority of the photometric variation is expected to be due to orbital
motion. Other combination frequencies are believed to come from interference between
the independent oscillations in the star. Resonance between two modes can produce an
artificial signal in the spectrum [38]. Any of these described peaks are not independent
and should not be considered when characterising pulsation [37].
Rotational Multiplets
Stars are known to be able to rotate, with a wide variety of possible rotational velocities.
In the context of stellar oscillations, rotating stars will depart from spherical symmetry and
non-radial oscillations will split into multiplets. The splitting depends on the degree of the
mode, such that the multiplet will consist of 2l + 1 frequencies [39]. Each peak belongs
to the same pulsation mode, and should thus not be identified as independent oscillation
frequencies.
2.6
Summary
In this chapter, a review of all the key theoretical concepts relevant for the understanding
of the project was given. This included firstly the definition of key stellar parameters and
spectral classes, the description of stellar evolution and introduction of the HR diagram.
Additionally, an introduction to chemically peculiar stars was given. Moreover, the basics
of stellar oscillations, from the physical understanding of these to the overview of current
observational knowledge of pulsating A & F stars were provided. Finally, the primary tools
and related challenges for analysing stellar oscillations were introduced.

3
Data
This chapter is dedicated to presenting the data that will be used for studying stellar os-
cillations in Am and Fm stars. It involves data from TESS and Gaia, as well as a catalog
of chemically peculiar stars from which the sample of Am stars is built.
3.1
Transiting Exoplanet Survey Satellite (TESS)
Earth-based observations are highly limited in quality due to the long temporal gaps in-
duced by the day-night cycle, as well as the influence of the atmosphere. Consequently,
space-born telescopes have revolutionised the study of stellar oscillations and asteroseis-
mology and continue to provide valuable data [1]. The Transiting Exoplant Survey Satellite
(TESS) is a satellite aimed at detecting exoplanet transits in nearby stars. Seen as the
next mission for exoplanet research after the Kepler satellite, it was designed to observe
bright stars that would enable follow-up characterisations of the discovered exoplanets
and was launched in 2017 by NASA [2].
3.1.1
Satellite Characteristics
TESS is located in a highly elliptical orbit with a period of 13.7 days to allow for long,
uninterrupted observations and efficient data transfer conditions. Four identical cameras
are mounted on the satellite, each observing different parts of the night sky. They have a
field of view 24 x 24 degrees, resulting in a combined wide field of view of 24 x 96 degrees.
The band pass filter used for TESS is plotted in Figure 3.1. It was designed to be most
sensible in the red region of the visible spectrum, in order to study stars in the spectral
range F5-M5, which are believed to be ideal targets for exoplanet studies [2].
Figure 3.1: TESS spectral response, from [2].
The primary objective was to carry out an all-sky survey of bright stars over 2 years of
nominal mission. Both hemispheres were divided into 13 sectors, each of which are ob-
served for 27.4 days (corresponding to two full orbits). The observing pattern, shown
in Figure 3.2, results in gradual sector overlap towards the ecliptic poles, which are ob-
served for longer duration over the 2 year period [2]. Since the end of the nominal mission
in 2019, TESS has continued the sky survey, having to date fulfilled 58 sectors.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
19
==PAGE:28==
Figure 3.2: a) Combined field of view of TESS. b) Illustration of the TESS observing
pattern. c) Estimated duration of observation of the different portions of the night sky.
Taken from [2].
TESS was planned to observe in two different modes. Full frame images (FFI) are ob-
tained with a cadence of 30 minutes. Additionally, flux measurements are recorded for a
list of 200.000 stars at a cadence of one measurement every 2 minutes. The latter results
in a Nyquist frequency of 360 c/d which, combined with the high photometric precision of
TESS [2], enables reliable studies of δ-Scuti stars, including high-order pulsations [40].
3.1.2
Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes (MAST)
The data obtained with TESS is accessible through the Barbara A. Mikulski Archive for
Space Telescopes (MAST). The data archive contains TESS FFI, and 2-minute cadence
lightcurves. Each file corresponds to observations during one TESS sector (≈27 days).
Multiple files are produced for targets that have been observed in different sectors. The
lightcurves are generated by the TESS Signal Processing Operations Center (SPOC) us-
ing simple aperture photometry (SAP). The flux is determined by summing the individual
values from pixels within an aperture which is chosen to ensure high signal-to-noise ratios
for each star [41].
An example light-curve is shown in Figure 3.3. The flux has been normalised and the
time is given in barycentric Julian days, which corresponds to the Julian Date corrected
for Earth’s motion in the Solar System (which introduces a time delay). This target has
been observed for two successive sectors and data from both of these is shown. Multiple
gaps can be seen in the lightcurve. Most of these appear periodically and are dedicated
to data transmission to Earth, which happens once per orbit. The first gap is larger and is
of different origin. From the above time series, an amplitude spectrum can be computed
and is shown in Figure 3.4. Only the frequency range 0-50 c/d is displayed to facilitate
interpretation, although the entire spectrum goes up to 360 c/d. The amplitude is given in
parts per thousand (ppt). It is near 0 for most of the spectrum with small variations due
to noise contributions, indicating that no oscillations at these frequencies are present in
the star. This is also the case above 50 c/d. At low frequencies and in the region around
12 c/d, the amplitude is significantly higher. As has been explained previously, data gaps
contribute artificially to the spectrum. The interruptions during each orbit, occurring with a
period of 13.7 days, are expected to produce a signal around 0.07 c/d, making the inter-
pretation of low frequencies difficult unless multiple sectors are combined to create long
time series. On the other hand, the frequency peaks at 12 c/d can be interpreted as stel-
lar oscillations. This example illustrates some of the contributions to be aware of when
20

Figure 3.3: Example TESS lightcurve for two sectors.
Figure 3.4: Example TESS amplitude spectrum obtained from the time series in Fig-
ure 3.3.
analysing spectra.
3.1.3
TESS Input Catalog (TIC)
For large-scale surveys such as the one carried out by TESS, a catalog of the observed
stars is needed for efficient information retrieval. The TESS Input Catalog (TIC) fulfills this
demand. It contains coordinates and stellar parameters for all stars for which lightcurves
have been produced [42]. All stars in the catalog are assigned a unique identification,
referred to as the TIC number. Example of parameters provided in the TIC are:
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
21
==PAGE:30==
• TESS TIC number, an identifier given for each observed star
• Right ascension and declination
• TESS effective temperature
• TESS apparent magnitude
• Cross-matches with other databases, such as the Gaia, KIC and TYC identifiers
The effective temperature provided can either originate from spectroscopic measure-
ments in literature, of from empirical relations used to derive a value for TESS targets that
do not have a good temperature estimate [42]. For validation of the estimation process,
the photometric estimations of temperature from TESS are compared to those provided
in the LAMOST spectroscopic survey. The results can be found in Figure 3.5, for stars
outside the galactic plane and those within 15deg of the galactic plane. The latter shows
larger disagreement with the LAMOST estimates, likely due to high extinction in that re-
gion. The values seem to agree for low effective temperature stars, which are the primary
targets for the TESS mission [42], although the TESS effective temperatures seem to be
slightly lower than LAMOST values for stars above 7000K.
Figure 3.5: Comparison between photometrically derived TESS effective temperatures
and those of the LAMOST spectroscopic survey, from [42].
Because of the large field of view for each camera mounted on TESS, it may occur that
the light from the target is contaminated by a nearby star in the sky. A contamination
value is associated with each target in the TIC, describing the fraction of contaminated
flux present in the aperture for the target [42].
The TESS Asteroseismic Science Operations Center (TASOC) is a data centre hosted by
the TESS Asteroseismic Science Consortium (TASC) at Aarhus University, in Denmark.
It is dedicated to the asteroseismic study of the stars observed with TESS. Dedicated data
processing pipelines for lightcurve have been processed for a handful of TESS sectors.
Importantly, TASOC provides a simple interface to query the input catalog an retrieve
valuable information for the targets of interest.
3.2
Gaia Astrometry
In many astronomical disciplines, precise knowledge of the position of celestial objects
is crucial to perform any data analysis. Astrometry is the study of stellar positions and
motions. One of the major tasks is the determination of stellar parallaxes, which is often
used for distance calculations.
22
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:31==
3.2.1
Satellite Characteristics
Gaia is an initiative from the European Space Agency (ESA) to study the structure and dy-
namics of the Milky Way [43]. The aim of this satellite is to measure the three-dimensional
positions and velocities of a large amount of stars, as well as determine their astrophysical
parameters and potential variability. Gaia was launched in 2013 and has since observed
over a billion stars in our Galaxy, revolutionising the quality and quantity of astrometric
data. It consists of three instruments [44]:
• Astrometric instrument
• Blue and Red Photometers (BP/RP)
• Radial Velocity Spectrometer (RVS)
The astrometric instrument will provide broadband observations in the entire visible range,
from 350 nm to 1000 nm. It will among other things provide Gaia magnitudes G and
parallax estimates for all observed stars. The BP and RP provide low-resolution spectra
with the BP being sensitive in the blue region of the spectrum, from 330 to 680 nm, and the
RP being sensitive in the red region, 640-1000 nm. Finally, the RVS will measure radial
velocities from light in the wavelength range 847-874 nm. A summary of the wavelength
ranges can be found in Figure 3.6. Gaia operates as a full-sky survey, observing all stars
brighter than the limiting magnitude of the satellite, corresponding to G = 20. Therefore,
data from Gaia is ideal for large surveys as the targets are not biased by selection [45].
Figure 3.6: Sensitivity of the different instruments. The Gaia G-band is shown in black,
the BP in blue, the RP in red, and the RVS in green. From [44].
3.2.2
Gaia Archive
All data from the satellite is made available in the Gaia Archive, which is an online database.
Earlier last year, the Gaia Data Release 3 (DR3) was made available to the public [3]. It
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
23
==PAGE:32==
contains data from the first 3 years of observations and provides access to a wealth of
data products through different tables, which will be discussed in the following sections.
A summary of the data release can be found in Table 3.1.

3.2.3
Astrophysical Parameters Inference System (Apsis)
The Gaia Data Processing and Analysis Consortium derives a wide variety of parameters
for targets stars using their Astrophysical Parameters Inference System (Apsis) [46]. It
contains several modules, which operate under different assumptions and produce esti-
mates for certain parameters. Some operate on subsets of the observed targets, and their
respective coverage in the HR diagram can be found in Figure 3.7. As can be seen in the
figure, these regions overlap, implying that Apsis can assign multiple values for a single
target. An overview of the estimated parameters is provided in Table 3.2. The following
sections are dedicated to presenting the modules that are relevant for the study of Am
stars.
Figure 3.7: Coverage of all Gaia modules, from [46].
24
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:33==

Table 3.2: Summary of derived stellar parameters in the Apsis modules.
General Stellar Parametrizers (GSP Phot/Spec)
Apsis contains two General Stellar Parametrizers (GSP) modules, for which a detailed de-
scription can be found in [46]. The photometric module (GSP-Phot) uses BP/RP spectra,
G-band magnitude and estimated parallax to estimate stellar parameters. The spectro-
scopic module (GSP-Spec) solely uses RVS spectra for estimations of certain parameters.
GSP-Phot has the widest application range, as seen in Figure 3.7, and determines val-
ues for most stellar parameters in Table 3.2. It provides the best homogeneous sample
of Gaia stellar parameters as a consequence [45].
Final Luminosity Age Mass Estimator (FLAME)
Luminosity, mass, radius and age of the target stars are computed based on inputs from
the GSP modules presented above, such as effective temperature and surface gravity
[46]. This is carried out by the FLAME module. It assumes solar metallicity when deter-
mining the stellar age.
Multiple Star Classifier (MSC)
In the above-mentioned estimations, the target was assumed to be a single star. In the
case of binarity, however, contributions from the companion are not taken into consider-
ation. This can affect the effective temperature considerably. The Multiple Star Classifier
(MSC) computes stellar parameters under the assumption of unresolved binarity, thus
returning one effective temperature measure for each star [46].
The Extended Stellar Parametrizer for Hot Stars (ESP-HS)
The Extended Stellar Parametrizer for Hot Stars (ESP-HS) infers the same stellar param-
eters for stars with effective temperatures greater than 7500K from both BP/RP and RVS
spectra, using other assumptions [47]. Solar composition is assumed. It is mostly tailored
towards O and B type stars, where the estimates from ESP-HS differ most significantly
from GSP-Phot [46].
3.2.4
Uncertainties
For all astrophysical parameters estimated through Apsis, the uncertainties are provided
as lower and upper confidence values in the Gaia Archive, representing a 68% confidence
interval [46]. However, these have a tendency to be underestimated for certain parame-
ters. For example, the effective temperature has unrealistically low uncertainties, of the
order of a few K, for photometric estimations such as those from GSP-Phot. Comparisons
with other catalogs result in a median absolute error of 110K, which is recommended in-
stead. Similarly, a recommended uncertainty of 0.2 dex is given for the surface gravity
values [45]. The values themselves seem to agree with literature, with best performance
noted for stars brighter than magnitude G = 16. On the other hand, it appears that the
log g values are slightly overestimated for main sequence stars. GSP-Spec does not com-
pute parameters for as many stars as GSP-Phot and analyses light emitted at different
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
25
==PAGE:34==
frequencies. Comparing the two modules is therefore difficult and may instead be use-
ful to complement each other, although the effective temperatures seem to agree for the
most [45].
 
The luminosity estimates provided by FLAME seem to agree with other estimates of the
same targets found in literature, with a slight tendency to over-estimate [45]. A dispersion
of roughly 6% is identified for the values. The ESP-HS module operating on hot stars
is suspected of underestimating the stellar parameters. The uncertainties increase with
temperatures and reach 300 K in A-type stars [45]. The Multiple Source Classifier’s perfor-
mance assessment on binary stars is less clear, with analysis of some data showing it has
similar or slightly better performance than GSP-Phot regarding effective temperatures. In
general, it is recommended to use the values of stars in binary systems cautiously [45].
3.2.5
Additional Tables
Aside from stellar parameters, some additional data products are of interest for charac-
terisation of Am stars. These are presented in this section.
Non-single Stars
Along with stellar parameter estimation, the data was also analysed for characterisation
of binary systems. In Gaia DR3, a small fraction of the binaries in the Gaia catalog was
analysed [48]. Four tables are provided and are listed below.
• Two Body Orbit: Contains parameters for two-body orbital solutions
• Acceleration Solutions: Provides acceleration information for stars with non-linear
proper motion
• Non-linear Spectroscopic Binaries: Contains information about trends in spectro-
scopic binaries
• Variability Induced Mover: Delivers solutions for photometrically variable binary sys-
tems
The first table contains solutions for spectroscopic, eclipsing binaries and astrometric bi-
naries and has enabled discoveries of many systems of the latter type [48]. Some selec-
tion criteria were introduced during the data analysis, a detailed description of this being
available in [48]. For studies of spectroscopic binaries, a minimal number of 10 transits is
required. Moreover, the allowed effective temperature range is 3875 - 8215 K. Goodness
of fit requirements are also imposed to remove spurious solutions.
External Tables
The Gaia archive also provides catalogs for the observed stars with estimations that do not
originate from the Apsis modules. An example of this are alternative distance estimates
using Bayesian probabilistic inversion [49]. These can be particularly useful for stars
where the parallax is poorly estimated, and the distance cannot be inferred from it directly.
3.3
Renson Catalog
The Renson catalog gathers information for more that 8000 chemically peculiar stars [6].
It is accessible through the Centre de Données astronomiques de Strasbourg (CDS). An
excerpt of the catalogue can be found in Table 3.3, where each row represents an en-
try for a star, providing its running number (Rsn), coordinates, spectral type, magnitudes
V and y for Johnson and Strömgren systems, and corresponding colour indices. Some
additional notes are provided in the last column, which may include information about for
example magnetic fields and variability [6]. The data provided in the tables are weighted
means of the values compiled from literature. In this table, two identifiers are provided
26
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:35==
for each target, the HD and Tycho (TYC) numbers, although others are available. The
Renson catalog contains 3652 Ap stars, 4299 Am and 162 HgMn stars. Known Fm and
ρ-Puppis stars are included in the Am category. Likewise, Bp stars are also marked as
Ap. λ-Boo stars are not included as their peculiarities originate from accretion.
The spectral class of each star, either Ap, HgMn or Am, is given in the fifth column. For
rapid identification, Am stars are given two classes according to their standard notation,
which are separated by a dash (-). In some cases, the second spectral class is not iden-
tified, and the dash is therefore followed by a blank space. The term Mn Hg is added at
the end of the column for HgMn stars. Ap stars are assigned a single spectral type and
the symbol of the chemical element that is most clearly over-abundant. Confidence esti-
mates regarding the assigned peculiarity are provided for some stars in the fourth column
of Table 3.3, they can be:
/ if the classification is wrong
? if the classification is uncertain
* if the classification is confirmed
The Renson catalog provides the longest list of Am stars to date. It can be used to create
a large sample of Am stars for statistical studies of oscillations in these stars.
3.4
Summary
In this chapter, the data relevant for analysing stellar oscillations in Am stars was pre-
sented. The Renson catalog provides the largest sample of chemically peculiar stars,
specifically Am and Fm stars. Lightcurves for these stars are obtained from the TESS
satellite, from which amplitude spectra will be computed in order to determine the exis-
tence and nature of the oscillations. The Gaia mission provides data for an unprecedented
amount of stars and the derived stellar parameters can be used to complement the anal-
ysis.
