4
Methods
This chapter presents the various methods used for creating the Am and Fm star data
set and characterising their pulsations. This project begins with a sample of over 4000
Am stars from the Renson catalog before any filtering has been applied. Due to the large
amount of stars considered and the even greater amount of data involved, most steps
needs to be automated to some degree and considerations must be made to facilitate this.
A schematic overview of the data acquisition and preparation is provided in Figure 4.1.
It is worth mentioning that unless stated otherwise, the code required to complete the
various tasks was written in Python 3.8.
Figure 4.1: Overview of the data acquisition process. Details regarding these steps are
provided in section 4.1 and section 4.2.
4.1
Am Sample Selection
The starting point for the creation of an data set of Am/Fm stars is the Renson catalog.
From it, all Am stars are identified by the dash present in their spectral type, and their coor-
dinates are retrieved. A quick investigation of the resulting table showed that no common
identifier is available in the Renson catalog. When working with many targets, it is most
convenient that all stars have a single system to refer to these. Not having this leads
to complications when handling large amounts of data, especially when cross-matching
needs to be done, either locally or with online databases such as MAST and the Gaia
Archive. The TIC numbers constitute the optimal system to keep track of all stars, as
they provide most flexibility. Indeed, the TESS Input Catalog can be used to search for
information on Am stars in multiple databases, such as MAST, TASOC and Simbad.
 
A positional cross-match is carried out with the coordinates obtained from the Renson
catalog, by querying MAST for sources at the corresponding right ascensions and dec-
lination for all stars. A narrow search radius of 5 arcseconds is used. When multiple
IDs are returned from the positional search, information for these is sought in the TASOC
database. All but one number are labelled as artifacts or duplicates and appear to have
significantly lower TESS magnitudes. These can thus be discarded and one final number
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
29
==PAGE:38==
is retained. Similarly, data download from the Gaia Archive is most efficient when using
the Gaia ID. Using the coordinates from the Renson catalog, another positional cross-
match was carried out, in order to retrieve the Gaia DR3 IDs.
 
When constructing both the TIC and Gaia target lists, some stars did not return any re-
sults, and were thus removed from the Am sample. One final filtering step was to consider
the amount of data available from the Gaia Archive for each target. Certain modules of
the Apsis pipeline only process stars that meet certain conditions (see subsection 3.2.2).
For completeness, targets for which no full parameter characterisation could be retrieved
were removed from the sample. This excludes stars that do not have values for effective
temperature, distance, absolute magnitude and log g from GSP-Phot, as well as luminos-
ity from FLAME.
4.2
Data Acquisition and Processing
Once the final target list is defined, data can be retrieved from the different databases. The
Gaia Archive allows for bulk downloads from its numerous tables through with commands
that are provided online. A pipeline was developed in order to retrieve astrophysical pa-
rameters for the target Am stars. TESS 2s cadence lightcurves for all the selected targets
are downloaded from the MAST archive. The processing steps of these time series is
discussed in the sections below.
4.2.1
Lightkurve
Lightkurve is a community-developed Python tool designed for analysing photometric data
from Kepler and TESS satellites [50]. The package makes use of astropy [51, 52, 53] and
astroquery [54]. It allows for download of data directly from MAST and was used to retrieve
lightcurves for stars in the Am target list. In some rare cases, only full-frame images were
available. Thus, stars with no time series data were excluded from the target list.
 
As MAST provides one file for each sector where a target is observed. These were all
downloaded and combined to produce a single, longer time series for each star to improve
the quality of the computed amplitude spectra. Lightcurves are then normalised for each
star, so that they are shown on the same scale and comparisons can be made. The zero-
point of all sectors is set to the same value. The mean flux is computed for each of these
and removed from all data points to shift all sectors towards a mean value of 0. The flux
is normalised and given in units of parts per thousand (ppt).
4.2.2
Lomb-Scargle Periodogram
The next step in the analysis of Am star pulsations is to compute the amplitude spectra
from the downloaded lightcurves. To this effect, the Lomb-Scargle periodogram, which is
part of the Fourier methods family, is used [55]. It assumes uncorrelated white noise, and
is directly implemented in Lightkurve. A periodogram is computed for all stars based on
the combined time series of all sectors. The frequency is given in cycles per day and the
amplitude in parts per thousand.
4.3
Am Classification
Having acquired the relevant data for studying stellar oscillations in Am stars, it is now
possible to proceed to the identification of which stars pulsate and thus which require
extraction of frequencies for a more detailed analysis. This is done through a classification
of variability involving different steps and iterations. A summary of the used pipeline is
given in Figure 4.2.
30
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:39==
Figure 4.2: Overview of the Am star classification process based on their amplitude spec-
tra. The first two steps correspond to the S/N filtering stages to eliminate truly variable
stars. The last step corresponds to the classification based on the shape of the spectrum,
the lightcurve and on contamination analysis.
4.3.1
Variability Determination
Firstly, the aim is to isolate the variable stars from the constant ones. The simplest way to
determine variability is to identify significant frequencies in the amplitude spectrum. It is
important here to not confuse true peaks, those related to pulsation, with spurious ones
that can occur from noise. The identification is based on the amplitude of the peak in
contrast to the background noise. For reliable detection and qualitative assessments, a
signal-to-noise ratio of S/N ≥4 is very commonly used [56].
To quantify the noise, a region of the spectrum devoid of pulsation peaks is preferable.
This is because the power from a pulsation can be spread over a range of frequencies
near the true frequency due to various effects discussed in section 2.5. Moreover, the
process should ideally be automated to allow for efficient processing of the many stars
present in the target list. Upon quick visual inspection of a handful of spectra, it is clear
that very few, if any, frequency peaks are expected to show up in the range 250-360 c/d
for all stars. This region is thus suited to determine a mean value for the noise in each
star’s amplitude spectrum. From this, a target is classified as variable if the frequency
with highest amplitude in the spectrum has S/N ≥4.
Based on this first iteration, all the constant stars were filtered out. A visual inspection
to validate the results showed many sources for which the spectrum contained signatures
that do not originate from pulsations, but still achieve a S/N greater than 4. Possible
explanations can be features introduced from gaps in data or contamination from other
sources. Some examples are shown in Figure 4.3. Constant stars indeed do not show
any distinct features in their amplitude spectra, while marginal variable stars have peaks
that satisfy the S/N criterion. However, in Figure 4.3b these signals are not due to intrinsic
variation from the star. Instead, such a signature matches the expected consequences of
large data gaps between sectors. The lightcurve for the displayed marginal variable star
does confirm this. It can be found in section A.2.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
31
==PAGE:40==
(a) Amplitude spectrum of a star classified as constant. No distinct signatures are visible.
(b) Amplitude spectrum of a star classified as marginally variable. It is not truly variable, although
a strong signal is present at low frequencies, mostly due to large gaps in data or trends in the
lightcurve.
Figure 4.3: Illustration of constant and marginal variable stars.
Consequently, a second filtering stage is necessary to remove these stars with ”marginal”
variability. A more strict S/N criterion of 10 seems to effectively select stars with variable
behaviour in the spectrum in automated pipelines [31]. This indeed removed all marginally
variable stars, with a few truly variable stars being misclassified. Manual verification of
the results helped identify these special cases.
4.3.2
Variability Characterisation
In summary, an automated analysis using the amplitude spectra was carried out in order
to filter out targets that are not suspected to pulsate. The confirmed variability in the
remaining stars is however not necessarily due to stellar oscillations, as variations in the
flux can be caused by a handful of other effects. The following variability classes are
considered:
• Pulsating stars
• Binary systems
• Modulated lightcurves and other variability
The first category is identified from the amplitude spectrum, with pulsations expected to
32
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:41==
be observed at low frequencies if a γ-Dor star is considered or at higher frequencies in
case of δ-Scuti pulsation. Binary systems can in some cases be identified both from the
lightcurve and the amplitude spectrum, depending on the configuration of the orbit. In the
case of eclipsing binaries, the lightcurve shows distinct, sharp dips in flux when one star
transits in front of the second, as in Figure 4.4a. The depth of the eclipses varies from
one star to another and may not be detected if they are too weak. A second indication
of eclipsing binaries can be found in the amplitude spectrum, where the variations induce
a clear structure, visually similar to that of a comb. An example is shown in Figure 4.4b,
where a series of evenly spaced peaks are visible, as a consequence of the non-linear
nature. Binaries with ellipsoidal variation can also be identified by a well-defined pattern
in the lightcurve such as the one in Figure 4.5. The lightcurves have a characteristic,
M-shaped periodic variation [57].
(a) Lightcurve of a star in an eclipsing binary. The eclipses are very clear, with large dips in
brightness during transits.
(b) Amplitude spectrum of a star in an eclipsing binary. Evenly spaced peaks of decreasing am-
plitude define the characteristic comb structure.
Figure 4.4: Characteristics of an eclipsing binary system.
The class of modulated stars includes Am stars for which periodic variations in brightness
are observed in the lightcurve, which is likely due to star spots on the surface. As the
star rotates, the brightness of the star decreases when the spot is on the hemisphere
facing Earth. This results in significant peaks being detected at low frequencies in the
periodogram. There is a risk of confusion between the ellipsoidal variation and the modu-
lation in the lightcurve, as the shape of the variation can look similar. However, because
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
33
==PAGE:42==
modulation is due to rotation, the velocity is physically limited by the break-up velocity of
the star. This translates to an upper limit for the frequency in the spectrum, of roughly 4
c/d. This is not the case of binary systems, and the frequency at which the peak occurs
can help identify the variability type.
Figure 4.5: Lightcurve showing ellipsoidal variability, characteristic of binary systems.
Classification is carried out manually. An important tool to facilitate this process is to
generate a visual catalog of the Am stars. It consists of one page for each star, in which
the following data products are displayed:
• A table containing Gaia astrophysical parameters
• The lightcurve of the star from one TESS sector
• The star’s entire amplitude spectrum, computed from the full time series
• Subdivisions of the amplitude spectrum to improve readability at low frequencies
and in the δ-Scuti pulsation regime
• The amplitude spectrum on logarithmic scale to aid in the detection of excited fre-
quencies
The catalog was inspected and each star was assigned to one of the above-mentioned
classes, over multiple iterations. Because there is no guarantee that non-eclipsing bina-
ries will show ellipsoidal variation, a literature search was carried out in parallel to search
for previously detected binary systems. If photometric variability is seen but binarity can-
not be confirmed, the star is added to the ”modulated and other variability class”. It is
worth noting that stars can be found in more than one category, as two types of variability
can coexist.
Due to the wide field of view of TESS, there is a chance of light contamination from a
nearby source. Consequently, it is possible that stars classified as pulsating in fact do not
pulsate, but rather that a neighbouring star does. Contamination verification is done in
two ways. Firstly, stars with few excited frequencies with extremely small amplitudes (of
the order of 0.01 ppt) are considered to be contaminated. Usually these stars also have
odd peak morphology, where these appear as single vertical lines, similarly to a Dirac,
see Figure 4.6. Such behaviour is not expected to be found in real spectra affected by
noise, and does in fact stand out from the spectra of other confirmed pulsating stars found
in the sample. Secondly, a contamination estimate for all pulsating stars is retrieved from
34
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:43==
TASOC and used to identify potential impostors. These are then looked up in TASOC for
sky images to search for nearby stars. Depending on the magnitude of the Am star and
those near it, the lightcurve of significant targets are obtained from MAST and the ampli-
tude spectrum is computed in Lightkurve. Neighbour stars were required to be of same
magnitude as the Am star or brighter in order to be considered. The spectra were then
compared to verify the presence of identical features. In the case of similarity, the star for
which the excited frequencies have the largest amplitude is considered as the host star.
Contaminated Am stars are removed from the target list.
Figure 4.6: Example amplitude spectrum of a contaminated star, that was perceived as
pulsating in the automated filtering processes. The amplitude of the peaks are small.
4.4
Frequency Analysis
Once the variable stars are detected and classified such that the pulsating Am stars can
be identified, the pulsations can be characterised.
4.4.1
Frequency Extraction
Frequency extraction, or pre-whitening, corresponds to determining the frequencies of
oscillations present in the lightcurve of the star. The Extraction of CoHerent Oscillations
program (ECHO), developed by Aarhus University, was used to perform iterative pre-
whitening [5]. The spectrum is obtained using a fast Lomb-Scargle periodogram tech-
nique, where Fast Fourier Transforms (FFT) are implemented in certain steps of the com-
putation to reduce the order of operations [58].
Frequencies are extracted from the periodogram using iterative sine-wave fitting [59]. The
method works by fitting a Fourier series with frequencies identified from the periodogram
to the time series data in order to determine the phase and amplitude. This is done for
one frequency at a time, starting with the frequency with highest amplitude as determined
from the spectrum. Once a fit is found, the corresponding signal is removed from the
lightcurve and the next frequency is fitted. The outcome of the algorithm is a list of fre-
quencies with associated phases and amplitudes. Because of the risk of large peaks due
to noise contributions rather than stellar oscillations, the statistical significance of each
peak is evaluated in the extraction process according to a false alarm probability [60]. A
power (or correspondingly, an amplitude) level z0 is defined such that false detections of
peaks only occurs in a fixed fraction of cases p0, as shown in Equation 4.1.
z0 = −ln(1 −(1 −p0)
1
N )
(4.1)
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
35
==PAGE:44==
N corresponds to the number of frequencies searched for. The level increases for smaller
values of p0. It is set to 0.0027 in this project to ensure high significance of the retrieved
frequencies. In addition to the assertion of statistical significance, the reliability of the
extracted signal is determined by comparing its amplitude with that in the original signal
before the extracted signal has been removed. This is a necessary step to avoid the intro-
duction of too many artificial signals that are statistically significant [61]. The amplitudes
are required to agree according to the relation given in Equation 4.2 [5].
α < Aextracted
Aoriginal
< 1
α
(4.2)
The value of α is important, as it has an influence on the number of false detections but
also defines the fraction of true peaks extracted from the spectrum. The recommended
deviation of α = 0.75 is kept, although it was tailored to frequency extraction of 4-year
Kepler lightcurves.
The uncertainties for the frequency, amplitude and phase of the extracted signals are
computed based on the following equations from [62].
σ(f) =

N is the number of data points, T the total length of observations, a the amplitude and
σ(m) corresponds to the root-mean-square deviation of the magnitude of the star due to
noise. These should only be considered as lower limits for the errors, as they assume
uncorrelated noise and evenly space data points in the time series.
 
The process described above is carried out over multiple iterations to optimise the ex-
tracted values and take into consideration the influence between peaks, by re-injecting
extracted signals in the time series and computing the amplitudes [5]. ECHO was used to
perform frequency extraction for all pulsating Am stars. A maximum of 500 frequencies
was allowed for each star, although this limit was never reached and in principle is set to
allow for extraction of all frequencies present in the spectrum. All peaks are optimised at
each iteration and the process is stopped once 7 consecutive failures occur. Failures can
be due to not meeting the significance or amplitude criteria discussed before, respectively
Equation 4.1 and Equation 4.2. In some rare cases the frequency extraction did not yield
a satisfactory result, despite clear, high-amplitude peaks being present in the amplitude
spectrum. The amplitude criterion was relaxed to circumvent this issue.
4.4.2
Combination Frequencies
Only independent frequencies are of interest in the analysis of stellar oscillations and
potential asteroseismic modelling. Therefore, it is important to assess the existence of
combination frequencies after pre-whitening. We recall from Equation 2.11 that combi-
nation frequencies are linear combinations of other frequencies. The complexity of the
combination frequency is given by the order O, that depends on the coefficients of the
linear combination, as shown in Equation 2.12. With the development of space-borne
telescopes, the high quality data have enabled detection of significantly more indepen-
dent frequencies. On the other hand, the risk of finding combination frequencies and
36
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:45==
therefore the chance of misinterpretation of data has also increased [37]. Correct iden-
tification of combination frequencies and harmonics is an important step to ensure the
validity of the subsequent analysis. Following the recommended practice from [37], only
combination frequencies of order O = 2, thus involving at most two parent frequencies,
are considered in the first place. The argument is that higher order combination frequen-
cies are unlikely to occur if there are no low-order instances. The combination frequency
search was carried using a module from ECHO. The identified peaks are discarded from
any further analysis.
4.4.3
Radial Mode Identification
As mentioned previously, mode identification is a complex task. Most Am stars showing
γ-Dor pulsation have been observed for 3 sectors or less and therefore possess too little
data for mode identification to be carried out. Consequently characterisation of δ-Scuti
pulsation is the main focus from hereon, specifically the radial modes, with degree l = 0.
The aim is to identify the radial fundamental mode for each star, as well as any higher
orders, to determine how many radial orders are excited in each star. The frequency of
these modes depends on the stellar parameters of the star, the effective temperature,
bolometric magnitude, and surface gravity and can be predicted using the pulsation con-
stant Q, as shown in Equation 4.6 [63].
log Q = −6.454 + log P + 0.5 log g + 0.1Mbol + log Teff
(4.6)
Where the bolometric magnitude Mbol = MG +BC is related to the absolute magnitude of
the star, taken from Gaia in this case, and a bolometric correction provided in Equation 4.7
[63].
BC = −8.499 · (log(Teff −4))4 + 13.421 · (log(Teff) −4)3
−8.131 · (log(Teff) −4)2 −3.901 · (log(Teff) −4) −0.438
(4.7)
In Equation 4.6, P corresponds to the period of the pulsation, and is the quantity of interest
as its inverse defines the frequency at which the radial fundamental and higher orders
can be expected for each star. Pulsation constants for low-degree p-modes, including
the radial modes, have been derived for different masses covering the A & F type mass
range [64]. The mean Q values for the radial fundamental mode (l = 0, n = 0) and
subsequent orders n > 0 are given in Table 4.1. Uncertainties are obtained from Monte
Order
0
1
2
3
4
5
6
7
Q
0.033
0.025
0.020
0.017
0.014
0.013
0.011
0.010
Table 4.1: Summary of expected values for the Q constant for different orders of the radial
l = 0 mode, from [64].
Carlo simulations. The errors for each stellar parameter in Equation 4.6 are assumed to
be Gaussian distributed, with mean value corresponding to the ones in Gaia and standard
deviation the recommended uncertainty given in subsection 3.2.4. Over 10.000 iterations,
the probability distributions for the three stellar parameters are sampled and a value for
the Q constant is derived. The final accepted value corresponds to the median of the
simulations, and the associated uncertainty to the standard deviation of all estimates.
4.5
Summary
In this chapter, the methods used for analysing Am stars were presented. The sample
selection based on the Renson catalog, as well as acquisition and processing of data from
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
37
==PAGE:46==
Gaia and TESS were described. The variability classification frequency extraction stages
were then detailed. Finally, the method adopted for computing the radial orders for each
star was introduced.
38
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:47==
5
Results
This chapter is dedicated to presenting the results from the analysis of Am/Fm stars based
on the Renson catalog and the TESS and Gaia satellites.
5.1
Am Catalog
When working with a large sample of stars, it is important to develop simple tools to quickly
and efficiently get an overview of each star. The first outcome of the Am sample creation
and data acquisition was therefore to develop an Am star catalog that compiles all poten-
tially relevant information for each target in a PDF. One page is dedicated to each star,
see below for an example of the layout. The contents chosen for this tool, from top to
bottom, are the following:
• The TIC and Gaia identifiers
• Information on preliminary variability analysis and number of sectors of data
• Gaia astrophysical parameters, with Gaia uncertainties, summarised in a table
• Lightcurve of the star in a single sector
• Full amplitude spectrum of the star
• Amplitude spectrum zoomed between 1-50 c/d and 50-360 c/d
• Amplitude spectrum in logarithmic scale
Only one sector of data is shown for each star to ensure that the variation in the lightcurve
can be seen, as some stars have large gaps between sectors, hence leading to com-
pressed images where no features can be identified. Because amplitudes can vary greatly
and the scale of the y-axis in all graphs is defined automatically by the highest amplitude
found, it is useful to show subsets of the spectrum for improved readability. The range 0-1
c/d is not shown because high amplitude peaks due to data gaps in this area can reach
amplitudes much larger than those of the pulsations. Most stars have pulsation peaks in
the range 1-50 c/d, which therefore was an optimal window to select for identifying pulsa-
tion. Higher frequencies are then gathered in the second window for completeness.
The red dashed line shows the limit of S/N = 4. It is computed for the entire interval
by the means of a sliding window with varying size. Because this limit should be used to
determine the significance of each peak, it needs a reliable estimate of the background
noise in the spectrum nearby. At low frequencies, many peaks are expected to be lo-
cated close together, so a small window is used to avoid the contribution of other peaks.
As the window moves towards larger frequencies, peaks become rarer and the back-
ground noise can be estimated more reliably with a larger window. This line is only used
to guide the eye when visually identifying significant peaks, which is useful to visually val-
idate the automatic variability classification of all the Am stars in the sample. Variability
classification was done by assuming a single value for the noise over the entire spectrum,
computed from the interval 250-350 c/d, where no peaks should be present to affect the
noise estimate.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
39
==PAGE:48==
==PAGE:49==
The pages for each star are generated individually, which allows flexible catalog genera-
tion by compiling only pages of stars of interest. In this way, catalogs of Am stars were
generated for the following categories:
• Constant stars
• Marginally variable stars
• δ-Scuti stars
• γ-Dor stars
• Hybrid stars
• Binary stars
• Modulated stars
• Contaminated stars
These catalogs are one of the major data products of this study and facilitated the anal-
ysis of such a large sample of Am stars, grouping the stars in categories of interest with
important properties and allowing for efficient inspection of these. They were used in the
first place for verification of the automatic variability determination by inspecting the am-
plitude spectra and identifying those with significant peaks. It was through this inspection
that the marginally variable class was defined, and the S/N = 10 significance criterion
was added to identify frequency peaks associated with pulsation. Secondly, the catalogs
were inspected to identify binary systems by looking for ellipsoidal variability or eclipses
in the lightcurves and comb structures in the spectra. Finally, the pulsating stars, filtered
from the binary systems and modulated stars showing no oscillations, were assigned a
class based on the observed peaks in the spectra, and the number of radial orders were
estimated in a slightly modified version of the layout shown above for stars which showed
p-modes pulsations. This last step will be discussed in greater detail in a later section.
 
Stars can appear in multiple catalogs if multiple characteristics are present, for example
p-mode and g-mode pulsations, or pulsational and binary behaviour. Contaminated stars
were also added to the marginally variable catalog, as the Am stars in these cases are con-
stant but appear variable due to a neighbouring star. All catalogs can be inspected on the
GitHub repository: https://github.com/zartalan/MasterThesis_AmStars. In order to avoid
uploading too heavy documents, the catalogs are split in multiple PDFs each containing
50 stars at most. The documents have the same name but are numbered differently.
5.2
Am Statistics
As an introduction to the study of this Am sample, all targets from the Renson catalog
are displayed in galactic coordinates in Figure 5.1. Those selected for further analysis
based on the above selections are highlighted in red. The Kepler field of view is shown
in blue. This illustrates the homogeneous sample of Am stars across the entire night sky
that has been obtained. Compared to Kepler, the TESS satellite allows observations of
considerably more Am stars and presents a unique opportunity to study their properties
in greater detail.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
41
==PAGE:50==
Figure 5.1: Overview of the distribution of Am stars in galactic coordinates. The galactic
center is located at the edges of the graph.
In this section, some general statistics on the Am stars in the sample are presented based
on the automatic variability determination and inspection of the catalogs. The key values
are presented in Table 5.1, where constant and marginally variable stars are gathered in
the same category ”constant”. This is done because the marginally variable stars are es-
sentially constant, but were flagged as variable originally due to spurious frequencies or
contamination from a neighbouring star. Out of the 4314 Am stars present in the Renson
catalog, 1287 of them, i.e. 30% of them, were found to have 2 second cadence observa-
tions with TESS and full parameter determination from Gaia. The latter selection criterion
resulted in the exclusion of some stars mostly due to the absence of luminosity estima-
tions from the FLAME module. Nevertheless, a sample of 1287 Am stars is sufficient to

infer some statistical properties of the Am population. It is found that almost one in four
of the considered targets pulsate, precisely 24%. However, the Renson catalog included
ρ-Puppis stars (also referred to as δ-Del stars) in their Am category. If these are not con-
sidered in the sample, as is done in [65], the final fraction of pulsating Am stars therefore
becomes 20%, which is the value provided in Table 5.1. This is slightly higher than what
is found in [65] with a smaller sample size, where only 12% of the sample consists of
pulsating Am stars. Regardless, this shows that a significant number of Am stars are able

==PAGE:51==
to pulsate.
77 stars were identified as having photometric variation but not clear pulsations, thus
labelled as ”modulated or other variability”. Because this variation is beyond the scope
of this project, no further investigation of the lightcurves to confirm or deny this has been
carried out. The TIC identifiers for these stars are provided in section A.3.
In general, the different classes shown in Table 5.1 were updated iteratively. After the au-
tomatic variability determination, lightcurve contamination analysis of variable stars was
carried out. Stars with a contamination value greater than 0.1 in TASOC were flagged,
and neighbouring stars were identified, also in TASOC. The lightcurves of these stars
were then obtained from MAST and amplitude spectra were generated to check for the
same peaks and compare their amplitudes. In total, 75 stars were considered for this
stage. Most stars were confirmed to be pulsating or were concluded to not be pulsat-
ing at all, and were added to the marginally variable target list. Only 16 stars showed
pulsation characteristics that originated from clear contamination from a neighbour star.
These were also added to the marginally variable stars and are considered as part of the
constant category in the above statistics. Overall few contaminated stars were identified
in the sample using this method.
5.2.1
Binary Incidence
The binary nature of stars was examined from the lightcurves and amplitude spectra for
all variable stars. Eclipsing binaries are easily identified, whilst ellipsoidal variability can
be confused with modulation from star spots. All suspected binary systems showing the
latter behaviour were included in a literature search with the help of TASOC search tools,
in an attempt to validate the binary nature. A target was confirmed to be binary if an
external source also marked it as binary using spectroscopic observations. In this way,
154 binary systems were found. Out of these, 73 are eclipsing binaries, corresponding to
a fraction of 6% from the entire Am sample. These results are summarised in Table 5.2.
 
As described previously, Gaia DR3 provides for the first time information about binary
star systems. The four data tables related to binary stars were investigated for information
related to Am stars. For the small subset of stars cross-matched in these tables, a handful
had radial velocity estimates but most were flagged as not having solutions. In their first
version, these tables therefore do not provide significant additional information for the
analysis of binary incidence in Am stars and are not considered in this study.

5.3
Gaia Parameters
Gaia DR3 provides a wealth of estimates of stellar parameters. This data was investigated
in order to get an understanding of the strengths and weaknesses of the different modules
and thus enrich the analysis of the final results. Most importantly are the luminosity and
effective temperature estimates, required to place the Am stars in the HR diagram.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
43
==PAGE:52==
5.3.1
Selection of Effective Temperature
Effective temperature values from different modules were retrieved from the Gaia Archive.
Some statistics are provided in Table 5.3. Both GSP-Phot and MSC1 modules provide
values for the entire dataset and therefore present the most interesting solutions for an
unbiased survey of Am stars. The two other modules only provide estimates for a sub-
set of Am stars. Nevertheless, the distribution of the effective values from the different
modules are examined to understand the impact on the location of stars in the HR dia-
gram. In Figure 5.2, the histograms from each module are compared to GSP-Phot, which
is considered the flagship module.

Figure 5.2: Distribution of effective temperatures for different Apsis modules in Gaia.
GSP-Phot is included in blue in all graphs. Left: comparison with the multiple star classi-

It appears to a first extent Gaussian distributed when considering the entire sample of Am
stars at low temperatures. A second hump around 10000K is however observed, resulting
in a weak bimodal character for the whole distribution. When comparing with the values
from the multiple star classifier, it appears that MSC1 predominantly assigns a discrete set
of temperatures to the targets. This results in an unphysical distribution of temperatures
when compared to GSP-Phot and leads to an HR diagram where stars are agglomerated
along vertical lines, corresponding to the temperatures with high occurrences in the figure.
This shows that although Am stars likely are in binary systems, the assumption of binarity
leads to effective temperatures that are not sensible in this module. In the second and
third graphs of Figure 5.2, only stars which were analysed by both modules are shown.
In these cases, it is clear that both GSP-Spec and ESP-HS modules do not have the
44
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:53==
same amount of hot stars above 8000K as does GSP-Phot. ESP-HS, which is applied to
hot stars only, seems to provide higher effective temperatures when compared to GSP-
Phot, while the distribution of GSP-Spec is similar, albeit with a larger tail towards lower
temperatures. Figure 5.3 shows the effective temperature estimates of both GSP-Phot
and GSP-Spec for each star. Just as can be seen in Figure 5.2, effective temperatures
are within a sensible range of values for A and F stars in GSP-Spec. For cooler stars,
below 8000K, the two modules provide values with less dispersion.
Figure 5.3: Effective temperature from GSP-Spot compared to that obtained from GSP-
Spec for all stars. Below 8000K, generally good agreement between both modules is
observed.
The high Teff provided by GSP-Phot seem unrealistic for A and F type stars, where GSP-
Spec provides more sensible values. However, for consistency, the temperature of all
stars is taken from the GSP-Phot module. This avoids introducing additional, unidentified
biases in the analysis, as might occur when using effective temperatures from GSP-Spec
or ESP-HS for a subset of stars. The ESP-HS module is best suited for estimations of O
and B type stars, and has higher uncertainties than GSP-Phot. Errors of 300K are very
large when placing stars in the HR diagram and reduces the significance of the analysis,
which supports the use of only GSP-Phot effective temperatures.
5.3.2
Validation of FLAME Luminosity
The luminosity obtained from Gaia is either inferred based on the parallax of the star or the
distance computed through GSP-Phot. In the case of poor parallax estimates, the lumi-
nosity has a tendency to be over-estimated. This is likely for fractional uncertainties of the
order of 15-20% [45]. Figure 5.4 shows the fractional uncertainty for the Am stars, which
in the vast majority is below 15%, meaning that there is little chance of over-estimation
due to poor parallaxes.
 
In parallel with the Gaia Early Data Release 3 (EDR3), distances to all stars were esti-
mated using a probabilistic approach, as distance estimates that follow from the simple
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
45
==PAGE:54==
inversion of parallax are poor if the parallax is not precisely known [49]. This made it pos-
sible to verify the distance estimates provided by Gaia by comparing the distance values
for all Am stars. Such a graph can be found in Figure 5.5. The geometric distance is com-
puted from the parallax and prior probability distribution for the distance, which depends
on the direction the star is located in [49]. Overall, there seems to be good agreement
between both method for this sample of stars, so the GSP-Phot distance appear valid for
luminosity determinations.
Figure 5.4: Fractional uncertainties for the parallaxes obtained from Gaia.
Figure 5.5: Comparison of distance obtained from GSP-Phot and a probabilistic approach
as described in [49].
In conclusion, the GSP-Phot stellar parameters are validated for use in the following anal-
ysis of Am stellar oscillations, although some clear limitations should be kept in mind when
interpreting the results.

Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars

5.4
Frequency Analysis
The output of variability determination is a list of 311 Am stars for which pulsation has been
confirmed in the amplitude spectra. In the following section, the frequency extraction and
analysis of obtained data is presented.

rejection process was therefore run a second time for roughly 20 stars, with more lenient
criteria. Results are gathered in a table for each star, as can be seen in Table 5.4. Fre-
quencies for all stars can be found in section A.4.

5.4.2
Pulsation Incidence
Based on the extracted frequencies, it is possible to determine the type of pulsation of
each Am star. The classification is based on the typical frequency range of γ-Dor and
δ-Scuti stars. High order g-modes are expected to be found below 4 c/d, while p-modes
mostly are found above this value. Hybrid stars shown pulsation peaks in both intervals. A
summary of the pulsation content of the Am star sample is provided in Table 5.5. The vast
majority of stars pulsate in p-modes, with only a small fraction of pure γ-Dor pulsators,
although g-modes are found in half the stars. The stars are placed in the HR diagram and
colour-coded according to the type of pulsation in Figure 5.6.

shown in solid black lines. The γ-Dor instability strip, from [32] is shown in dashed black
lines. Evolutionary tracks are included to facilitate the interpretation of the plot. These are
based on Warsaw–New Jersey models and assume no rotation and solar metallicity [66,

Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars

Binary stars are indicated with another symbol to distinguish them from supposedly sin-
gle stars. Constant Am stars are plotted in gray to give an idea of the entire sample of
stars. ρ-Puppis stars are also included in the diagram, and are marked with an orange
half-circle to distinguish them from pulsating Am stars. The γ-Dor and δ-Scuti instability
strips are shown in dashed and solid black lines, respectively. Because a large range of
temperatures are covered, as can be seen in Figure 5.6a, the region of high data point
density within the instability strip is difficult to read and a second version of the HR dia-
gram is shown in Figure 5.6b where outliers have been ignored. The typical uncertainty
associated with all stars is given in the bottom of the graph.
Most pulsating Am stars are well within their instability strip, with some clear outliers
located outside of these, above 9000K. These will be discussed further in a following
section. Most δ-Scuti are concentrated near the red edge of the instability strip. Quite a
few γ-Dor stars are located far from the instability strip, and would have to be investigated
further to confirm the g-mode pulsations as they may have been misidentified because
of short time series. Hybrid stars are almost all located in the narrow region between
the blue and red edges of the γ-Dor and δ-Scuti instability strips, respectively. Overall, it
seems most pulsating Am stars in the sample are located close to the main sequence, and
would therefore be younger stars. ρ-Puppis stars are clearly located further away from
the main sequence, which is sensible if the assumption that they are evolved Am stars is
true. Generally, it does seem like the distribution of both classes of pulsating stars match
well in the HR diagram.
5.4.3
Range of Pulsation
In the remaining sections, focus is laid on the analysis of the p-mode pulsating Am stars,
as most γ-Dor stars lack enough observations to clearly resolve the g-modes and can
therefore not be analysed in detail. Figure 5.7 depicts the range of excited frequencies
in all p-mode pulsating stars, i.e. pure δ-Scuti and hybrid Am stars. Each star consists
of a column, and are ordered in increasing effective temperature. The excited frequency
range is showed in yellow. It is defined from lowest and highest frequency of pulsation, but
does not imply that all frequencies within this range are excited. From this it is seen that
cool Am stars, with temperatures below 7000K have relatively narrow ranges of excited
frequencies that tend to be around 5-20 c/d. For increasing temperatures, and especially
from 7300K and on, the ranges become broader and extend to higher frequencies.
5.4.4
Radial Orders
As a final step in the analysis of p-mode pulsations in Am stars, the number of excited
radial orders is determined using the method from subsection 4.4.3. The quantities are
added to the amplitude spectra in a modified version of the Am catalog that shows the ex-
tracted frequencies. In this way, the predicted values for the radial orders can be visually
compared to the actual pulsations present in the star. An example of the catalog layout is
provided at the end of this chapter. The extracted frequencies and identified combination
frequencies are shown in the amplitude spectrum in red and blue dashed peaks, respec-
tively. The black vertical lines correspond to the predicted locations of the radial orders.
The spectrum is split into three different intervals. The catalog of pulsating Am stars with
extracted frequencies and radial orders can be examined on GitHub.
From Monte Carlo simulations, the uncertainty intervals for each radial order overlap con-
siderably, rendering the analysis of these impossible. Upon investigation of the different
contributions to the overall error, it is found that the uncertainty of 0.2 dex for the surface
gravity suggested by the Gaia data validation [45] is the major contributor. Figure 5.8 il-
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
49
==PAGE:58==
Figure 5.7: Range of excited frequencies in p-mode pulsating Am stars. Each column
shows the frequency range from 0 to 80 c/d. The yellow intervals correspond to the excited
frequencies. Dark blue intervals are frequency ranges with no pulsation. The x-axis shows
the effective temperature.
lustrates this impact, where Figure 5.8a shows the uncertainties obtained from the Monte
Carlo simulations. If no errors are assumed for either the absolute magnitude or the ef-
fective temperature, no significant difference is observed. In Figure 5.8b, however, the
intervals shrink considerably when the uncertainty on the value of the surface gravity is
neglected. In order to include uncertainties in the Q constants, it would therefore be nec-
essary to reduce the contribution from the surface gravity by a large amount. Currently, it
will be assumed that the central values are accurately determined and the uncertainties
are ignored, as seems to be the practice in other studies [63].
The number of excited radial orders is then determined by inspecting the modified cata-
logs. An order is considered to be excited if significant pulsation peaks are found in the
interval between the black line determined from the Q constant of the same order and the
next line. If all orders are excited and additional peaks are found at higher frequencies, the
star is considered to have more than 8 orders excited. The results from this analysis are
summarised in the form of an HR diagram, shown in Figure 5.9. Each star is represented
by a colour-coded marker describing the number of excited orders, and ticks oriented in
a specific pattern to show which radial orders are identified in the amplitude spectrum.
The pattern is given in the bottom left of the graph, with numbers 0 to 7 representing the
fundamental up to the 7th overtone. It appears that most stars near the main sequence
have a significant amount of radial orders excited. Stars with more than 8 radial orders
excited are however scatter across the instability strip. Fewest radial orders are excited
near the red edge of the δ-Scuti instability strip. For a few stars, no radial orders were
excited, meaning that the peaks observed at frequencies below the expected position of
the radial fundamental either correspond to low order g-modes or mixed modes.
50
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:59==
(a) Q values and corresponding uncertainties obtained from Monte Carlo simulations. No signifi-
cant difference is observed when assuming no error in the values of the absolute magnitude and
effective temperature.
(b) Q values and corresponding uncertainties obtained from Monte Carlo simulations when no
error is assumed for the surface gravity.
Figure 5.8: Amplitude spectrum of a pulsating Am star, with predicted radial orders and
associated uncertainties.
It is now possible to compare the observed pulsation characteristics with theoretical pre-
dictions of Am pulsation. Turbulent pressure in the HeI/HI ionisation layer is thought to be
the main driving mechanism for pulsations in Am stars. Simulations of He-depleted outer
layers have been carried out in order to prove that this mechanism can induce pulsa-
tions and predict the location of these in the HR diagram [5]. Am stars are modelled with
reduced helium abundance, Y = 0.15 instead of 0.28. Figure 5.10 shows the predicted
number of radial orders excited based on these simulations. Excitation is the highest in
a region centered near 7500K and a luminosity of 1.5R⊙. Towards the red edge of the
instability strip and the main sequence, the amount of excited radial orders decreases.
The hotter region is expected to be devoid of pulsation.
When considering the observational HR diagram from Figure 5.9, it is clear that there
is a dearth of pulsating Am stars in the region oh high radial order excitation, which con-
trasts with theoretical predictions. Most Am stars have less than 8 radial orders excited,
and these are located closer to the main sequence, in fact outside of the region covered
by the simulations. In agreement with Figure 5.10, no or very few Am stars pulsate to-
wards the blue edge of the instability strip, and fewer radial orders are excited near the
red edge. For this analysis, the ρ-Puppis stars are ignored as the κ-mechanism is thought
to have a major role in stellar oscillations. As these have been located in Figure 5.6a, it
is possible to focus solely on the distribution of Am stars in the HR diagram.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
51
==PAGE:60==
Figure 5.9: HR diagram of pulsating Am stars showing the excited radial orders. The
colours indicate the number of excited orders while the ticks oriented around the central
marker indicate which orders were identified, following the numbering scheme given in
the bottom left of the graph. The typical uncertainty for all stars is given at the bottom as
well. All lines have the same significance as described in Figure 5.6.
Figure 5.10: Predicted number of excited radial orders assuming turbulent pressure in
the HeI/HI ionisation layer, taken from [5]. The evolutionary tracks are computed just as
explained in Figure 5.6. The instability strips are identical, with another another set of
theoretical blue and red edges for the δ-Scuti instability strip being shown in solid lines,
see [5].
52
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:61==
5.5
Summary
In this chapter, the results obtained from the data analysis were introduced. The Am cat-
alog used for visual inspection of the stars was presented, and key statistics of the stellar
population considered were summarised. A significant fraction of Am stars are found to
pulsate. Frequencies were extracted in order to characterise the pulsation. Stars con-
taining p-modes were analysed further to determine the number of radial orders excited
and comparison with theoretical predictions was made.
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
53
==PAGE:62==
==PAGE:63==
6
Discussion
In this chapter, some core results and assumptions related to the study of stellar oscilla-
tions in Am stars are discussed in further details, focusing on the limitations and future
work that can be done to build upon the contents of this report.
6.1
Am Star Sample Representativeness
A sample size of 1287 stars is significant enough to carry out a statistical analysis. How-
ever, one major concern is whether or not the sample is representative of the Am popu-
lation. This requires that potential biases be identified. Firstly, the spectral classification
provided in the Renson catalog contains some uncertainty.
In fact, several stars are
marked with a question mark if the spectral classification is doubtful. This was not taken
into account in the target selection, which means that there is a high chance that some
stars or a significant portion of the target list are not truly Am stars. Additionally, the cata-
log includes ρ-Puppis stars in the Am class. If they are suspected to be evolved Am stars,
this is not necessarily the case [22]. Although most of these are clearly more evolved and
can be separated visually from Am stars in the HR diagram, non conclusions can be made
regarding the most likely theory. Their inclusion in the statistics may have biased the re-
sults presented in the previous chapter and this should definitely be changed if ρ-Puppis
stars turn out to not be related to Am stars. The driving mechanism for pulsations in these
stars is expected to be the κ-mechanism, further confirming that these stars should not
be included when assessing the effect of turbulent pressure on stellar oscillations.
Secondly, the sample may be biased due to the target selection from TESS and Gaia.
All stars in this study were required to have at least one sector of data obtained from
TESS and full parameter estimation in Gaia. The former satellite is known to be mostly
sensible to bright stars, as the goal is to be able to detect small transiting exoplanets [2].
Gaia produces estimates of stellar parameters for faint stars, down to a limiting magnitude
of G < 20, although some parameters are only computed if measurements are of good
enough quality [45]. This has lead to removing multiple stars from the target list. The spa-
tial distribution of the sample of stars seems sensible based on Figure 5.1, although there
is a region away from the galactic center where no stars made it through target selection.
This is likely a bias due to the satellite scan-laws, and will hopefully be solved in the future.
Generally, very little information is available for the considered targets, apart from the
Renson catalog and the estimations from Gaia. Consequently, it is difficult to verify any
doubts regarding the spectral classification, binarity or stellar parameters. This also im-
pedes modelling of these stars to better understand the processes that occur. There is
hence a strong need for spectroscopic surveys of these chemically peculiar stars, which
would help unveil some of their mysteries regarding stellar oscillations.
6.1.1
Binarity
In another study [19], 70 eclipses were found from a sample of 1742 stars, or 4% of the
sample. This number is consistent with a binary fraction of 60-70% in Am stars. The re-
sults presented in this report indicate a slightly higher fraction of binary stars detected, with
73 cases identified in the sample of 1287 resulting in a fraction of 5%. The binary fraction
is determined by computing the detection probability from the SuperWASP observatories
[19]. Similar calculations could be made to determine the TESS detection probability and
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
55
==PAGE:64==
infer the equivalent precentage for this Am sample. In any case, both results show that a
significant fraction of Am stars, in the range of 70%, are in binary systems.
Very few samples are included in the Gaia non-single star tables but the sample is incom-
plete because only the most significant targets were retained [3, 69]. For spectroscopic
binaries, a minimum of 10 transits were required and the temperature range 3875-8125K
was considered [69]. Thus, the remaining Am stars may still truly be binary systems. Gaia
only analysed unresolved binaries, and data is only made available for stars where the
solutions fit well. Targets that have been considered in the analysis but were rejected due
to poor fits are not mentioned anywhere. It could be in future data releases that these non-
single star tables will include more Am stars, and that these can provide some valuable
information.
6.2
Data
In this section, the validity of the data used to analyse the Am star sample, most im-
portantly the Gaia parameters and the reliability of classification of certain groups, are
discussed.
6.2.1
Gaia Stellar Parameters
Stellar parameters from the GSP-Phot and FLAME modules were used in order to anal-
yse the Am stars. Alternatives for GSP-Phot do exist, although the assumption of binary
stars did not yield sensible effective temperature estimates for the sample, and other
modules did not analyse the entire target list. Under the assumption of high quality paral-
lax or distance values, the stellar luminosity and absolute magnitude should be correctly
estimated. Because Am stars are expected to be in binary systems, estimates from GSP-
Phot are likely incorrect [45]. These parameters are expected to be the main source of
uncertainty because they play a major role in the analysis of pulsation, as the effective
temperature and surface gravity are crucial for placing the star in the HR diagram and
determining the number of excited radial orders. Both MSC and ESP-HS modules as-
sume solar metallicity when deriving stellar parameters of stars, which in principle does
not apply to chemically peculiar Am stars and further supports the use of GSP-Phot val-
ues despite the assumption that the star is single [46]. Additionally, all modules process
mean spectra and astrometry, which in case of high variability may not be representative
of the star [46]. In the case of stellar oscillations in Am stars, this effect should probably
not be too significant.
 
As was noticed in Figure 5.6a, several stars are located outside the instability strip and
are labelled as pulsating Am stars due to their high effective temperatures, which is highly
unexpected. A more detailed literature search for these outliers did not provide additional
information, as most targets have not been subject to a detailed study before. One prob-
able explanation is that the effective temperatures are overestimated because the stars
actually are located in binary systems. In that case, Teff will experience a contribution
from both stars and thus be measured as warmer. Based on the values obtained from
different modules in Gaia, see Figure 5.2, this does seem plausible as no stars with tem-
peratures around 10000K are found in GSP-Spec and ESP-HS. When looking at the TESS
temperatures, it appears that the same trend is seen. Using the values from the GSP-
Spec module in the Gaia data could possibly have corrected this phenomenon. However,
this introduces an additional source of bias in the sample and was therefore not attempted.
In summary, spectroscopic observations of at least a subset of the Am stars would be
a sensible step in order to understand the outlier stars and validate the results of the anal-
56
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:65==
ysis of the rest of the sample. Key aspects of such a campaign would be to redetermine
the effective temperature reliably and verify in these stars are present in binary systems.
6.2.2
Analysis of Am Catalog
Some limitations regarding the preliminary analysis of variability should be mentioned.
Firstly, the stars classified as having modulated lightcurves, probably due to stellar spots,
should be verified more thoroughly. The morphology can easily be confused with that of
ellipsoidal variability in binary systems. If the lightcurve is folded with the main period, a
discontinuity should be observed for stars where modulation occurs. This is not the case
for binary systems, where the successive periods will match perfectly because the varia-
tion is governed by the orbital motion. Stellar spots on Am stars are interesting to study
further because they could be evidence of magnetic fields, which as stated before have
only been directly detected in a few stars. The TIC identifiers can be found in section A.3.
Secondly, there is a chance that not all contaminated stars have been found with the
adopted approach. Such stars are important to identify and remove from the statistics.
The TASOC contamination ratio gives a first indication in order to find the strongest cases,
but some stars may have been overseen. In principle, this could be investigated in greater
detail to avoid false positives. In case of binary systems, it is not possible to be sure that
the pulsations originate from the Am star or the companion. The confirmed contaminated
stars do however provide an indication on the overall uncertainty for the number of pul-
sating Am stars, so that the final result becomes 311 ± 16 pulsating stars in the sample.
6.3
Frequency Analysis
This section is dedicated to discussing the frequency extraction process, and all analysis
of stellar oscillations in Am stars that follows.
6.3.1
Frequency Extraction
When inspecting amplitude spectra, it was seen that the large data gaps in some lightcurves
of stars affected the peaks in a similar way to what was shown in Figure 2.7. As a con-
sequence, the exact pulsation frequency can be difficult to estimate precisely. Secondly,
long term trends in the satellite observations can induce false peaks at low frequencies,
which affects g-modes especially [70]. The lightcurve extraction and processing is thus im-
portant to remove these trends. This was not considered in the study, as most lightcurves
did not show clear trends and a large amount of data needed to be processed. It might
be relevant to have a closer look at this in the case of detailed modelling using the ex-
tracted frequencies, especially when considering γ-Dor pulsators for which the pulsations
are found in similar frequency ranges.
 
In this study, the extracted frequencies were used to determine the number of excited
radial orders and the range of excitation for all stars. This did not require the use of all
individual frequencies, but rather a consideration of the global pulsation characteristics.
Thorough validation of the extracted frequencies is however crucial in a case where the in-
dividual frequencies are needed and where ambiguous peaks can affect the interpretation,
such as in asteroseismology. The method of extraction and observational uncertainties
can have a major impact on the outcome of modelling [70].
Another important aspect is the identification of combination frequencies. Following the
procedure described in [37], only simple combinations of frequencies are considered, as
it is unlikely that higher orders occur if no combination frequencies are identified. It is rec-
ommended to include a comparison between observational data and simulations to de-
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
57
==PAGE:66==
termine if the combination frequencies are significant or just coincidental [37]. If these low
order combination frequencies are confirmed, higher order combinations can be sought
for. For simplicity, only low-order combination frequencies were considered in this study.
In case of binary systems or rotating stars, long series of harmonics are expected to
occur, which was indeed seen in all eclipsing binaries of this sample. However, not all
of them were identified in the automatic processing, showing the need for a more thor-
ough analysis for further work on Am stars. The harmonics in the eclipsing binaries are
moreover of high amplitude, which made the analysis of g-modes and low order p-modes
challenging, as these have generally lower amplitudes and may be impossible to iden-
tify. The signature should be removed to enable a full characterisation of the pulsation
spectrum in these stars.
6.3.2
Radial Order Uncertainties
Mode identification is a challenging task, specifically because stellar interiors and excita-
tion mechanisms are not fully understood. δ-Scuti stars display very different amplitude
spectra despite having similar stellar parameters, which hints at an unknown mode selec-
tion mechanism that can be induced by rotation, magnetic fields, diffusion, a combination
of these or something else. Therefore, the simplest approach to mode identification was
carried out in this project, involving the computation of expected frequencies for radial
orders with degree. Modes of higher degree l can be split by rotation and their identifica-
tion is therefore more challenging. Because Am stars are perceived as slow rotators, no
investigation of splitting was considered.
Furthermore, the number of excited radial orders is determined visually by deciding whether
significant peaks are present or not in the different intervals between the predicted orders.
It can be done quite effectively if the values obtained from the Q constants are correct,
with an average error of ±1 radial order. This is however not certain, as the uncertain-
ties are extremely large and the computations are done using Gaia stellar parameters,
for which limitations have already been discussed. Treatment of uncertainties is lacking
in this regard and should definitely be improved to improve the reliability of the analysis.
One major step in this direction is determining surface gravity with smaller errors, as has
been illustrated in Figure 5.8b.
At high radial order p-modes, mode identification becomes clearer as there is conver-
gence towards the asymptotic regime. However, few stars seemed to reach this in the
amplitude spectra, so no substantial effort was put into this. Identifying stars in this regime
and determining the large frequency separation would be valuable for the understanding
of Am stellar oscillations as it is related to the density of the star.
6.3.3
Discrepancy with Theoretical Predictions
When adding the information about the number of radial orders excited to the HR diagram
and comparing with predictions [5], it is clear that the models used for simulations do not
reproduce the observational data. The primary issue is the lack of pulsating Am stars in
the region of the HR diagram where high radial orders are predicted as a consequence of
turbulent pressure in the HeI/HI ionisation layer. This raises the question of whether the
incidence of pulsating Am stars in this area is truly low, which would clash with the current
model, or if some bias effect in the sample causes the difference. The former would indi-
cate that the mechanism causing pulsation operates in a different way. The latter could
be due to a selection bias that excluded stars in this area, or because position of stars
in the HR diagram is wrong. This could be because unresolved binary systems lead to
58
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:67==
overestimations of stellar parameters, as the measured values correspond to contribu-
tions from both the Am star and the companion. Better estimates could lead to a shift in
the position of some stars in parallel to the main sequence, and may change the overall
look of the diagram. Near the main sequence, where most pulsating Am stars have been
identified, up to 8 radial orders seem to be present in the observed Am stars. This is so
far not explained by the turbulent pressure simulations, as the number of excited orders
decreases quickly towards the main sequence.
 
There could be several explanations for why the theoretical predictions do not match the
data. The simulations are carried out in one-dimension, which is a very simplified ap-
proach and may oversee certain aspects. It is however a complex matter to perform three-
dimensional simulations and implement a sensible model for convection in that case. They
may also be affected by the fact that the chemical peculiarities in Am stars are observed
at the surface of these but that there currently is no knowledge about the interior structure
of Am stars. It may be that the internal abundances are different from those seen in the
outer layers, which can also affect stellar oscillations.
6.4
Future Work
In conclusion of this chapter, some considerations for future work are addressed.
6.4.1
Spectroscopic Follow-up at the Nordic Optical Telescope
As has been mentioned multiple times now, there exist a number of uncertainties regard-
ing Am stars which can affect the analysis significantly, such as lacking binary information,
biased effective temperatures and possibly wrong spectral classifications taken from the
Renson catalog. Most Am stars in the sample are only mentioned in the Renson catalog.
This lead to writing an observing proposal for the Nordic Optical Telescope in order to
characterise Am stars spectroscopically and try to answer some of the questions raised.
28 hours of observing time with the high-resolution FIbre-fed Echelle Spectrograph (FIES)
were allocated to this proposal for a total of 45 targets. The aim is to determine radial ve-
locities and effective temperatures, as well as verify the supposed Am star nature. The
data will also be made available for the Am star community to perform chemical abun-
dance estimates.
6.4.2
Study of the γ-Dor Pulsators
In this project, γ-Dor Am stars were simply identified, but no additional work was done
to characterise these. One reason is the lacking number of TESS sectors for these stars
which makes it difficult to resolve the oscillation frequencies. This also implies that some
may have been misclassified as γ-Dor pulsators and it would therefore be good to verify
this result. These stars nevertheless represent an interesting sample, as g-mode oscilla-
tions are driven by another region of the star and provide insight into the near-core region
of Am stars. In the case of hybrid stars, both g-modes and p-modes can be studied and
such stars are expected to be valuable in understanding the internal structure of stars.
With a relatively large sample of γ-Dor Am stars, it should be possible to study the inner
regions of Am stars in the near future, when a sufficient number of TESS sectors can be
combined.
6.4.3
Asteroseismology
So far only radial stellar oscillations have been studied in order to assess the number
of excited radial orders in stars for which p-modes have been identified. Much more
information is contained in the pulsation spectra of these stars, however, and the analysis
can be pushed further in future work through modelling of the stars. Identification of more
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
59
==PAGE:68==
modes and using these for asteroseismology can provide information on the excitation
mechanisms in Am stars and make it possible to constraint the interior structure of these.
60
Study of Stellar Oscillations in Am Stars
==PAGE:69==
7
Conclusion
In this project, a large sample of Am stars was studied in order to characterise stellar
oscillations of these unexpected pulsators. Taking as starting point the Renson catalog,
a total of 1287 stars were analysed with photometric data from the TESS mission. Stellar
parameters were obtained from the Gaia archive. The data was used to develop a cat-
alog of Am stars, from which the variability was determined and the pulsation spectrum
analysed. Key statistics were computed for the targets in order to provide insight into the
properties of this stellar population. 20% of Am stars are seen to be pulsating, and the
binary incidence is confirmed to be quite high, around 70% of the population. Frequency
extraction using the ECHO pipeline made it possible to determine the number of excited
orders and compare these with theoretical predictions assuming turbulent pressure as the
excitation mechanism.
 
The predictions do not match the observational data, as Am stars pulsating in high radial
orders are primarily located close to the main sequence. Despite uncertainties regarding
potential biases in the sample, it appears clear that turbulent pressure as it is modelled
now cannot account for the Am pulsations alone. Further work is necessary to investigate
what processes can explain the pulsational behaviour of Am stars.
There is a serious need for spectroscopic observations of Am stars, as several elements
provide uncertainties in the analysis of stellar oscillations. Such data can be used to de-
termine reliable effective temperatures, assess the potential binary nature and confirm
the chemical peculiarities. An observing proposal was accepted at the Nordic Optical
Telescope, granting 28 hours of observations for 45 stars spread over the period of April
to September 2023 with the FIES instrument. This should provide precise stellar param-
eters and additional information, serving as a valuable complement to either validate or
disprove the conclusions presented here.
